Background: Results of split liver transplantation (SLT) with pediatric donors have not been specifically reported, whereas the use of donors aged 10 years or less is currently not recommended. Patients and methods: From October 1997 to August 2003, on a total of 186 split liver procedures performed by the Bergamo transplant team alone or in collaboration with other liver transplant centers, 39 (21%) were carried out using donors aged < 15 years. In all cases the procedure generated a left lateral segment (segments II-III [LLS] ) and an extended right graft (segments I+IV-VIII [ERG] ). In 3 cases, no suitable recipient was found for the ERG and only the LLS was transplanted. Criteria for splitting were donor hemodynamic stability with normal liver function, and dimensional matching based on donor-to-recipient weight ratio. In all but one case the celiac trunk was maintained with LLS. Recipient population included 47 children receiving 39 LLS and 8 ERG and 28 adults transplanted with ERG. SLT were performed with 16 donors ≤ 10 years of age (mean weight 31 ± 9 kg) and with 23 between 11 and 15 years (mean weight 57 ± 11 kg). There were no differences among groups in pre-transplant patient status, cause of liver failure, primary or secondary SLT, and procurement parameters. Results: median recipients follow up was 19 months . Two-year patient and graft survival are reported in the table. Arterial complications (AC) occurred in 6% of the patients receiving split liver graft from pediatric donors (8% in the ≤ 10 years donors group, and 5% in the 10-15 years donors group). Recipient of LLS developed AC in 3% of the cases, while ERG recipients had AC in 12% of the cases. The incidence of surgical and infectious complications was also similar among groups. Conclusion: the use of pediatric donor for SLT allows to obtain good results, comparable to those obtained with SLT using adult donors.The similar incidence of AC among patients receiving LLS or ERG further supports the policy of maintaining the celiac trunk with LLS. Background/Aim: Biliary anastomotic complications or chronic rejection after liver transplantation may result in bile duct stenosis leading to liver graft failure and may require re-transplantation. We fabricated an artificial bile duct that would allow replacement of the native bile duct through tissue engineering techniques. Materials and Methods: Bone marrow cells taken from the swine were seeded onto the interior of tubular scaffolds of bioabsorbable polymer. These were scaffolds made from a porous copolymer of polycaprolactone and polylactic acid and reinforced with polyglycolic acid fibers. Hybrid pigs served as recipients of the bile duct organoid units (BDOU) fabricated by tissue engineering techniques. One hour after seeding the scaffolds with bone marrow cells, the bile duct organoid units were transplanted into each of the pigs from which bone marrow cells had been collected. Under general anesthesia, the animals were laparotomized and the common bile duct was cut around the confluence with the cystic duct. The duodenal end of the common bile duct was ligated, while the hepatic stump of the native common bile duct was anastomosed to the BDOU. Then, a hole, 5 mm in diameter, was made in the descending duodenum, to which was sutured the remaining end of the BDOU. The neo-bile duct was recovered 6 months after transplantation and subjected to macroscopy and histology. Results: Three recipient pigs survived up to the sixth month after transplantation, when they were sacrificed and their neo-bile ducts recovered. They gained weight after operation with no evidence of jaundice. Biochemistry performed just before sacrifice was within the normal range for all parameters examined in all three animals. On macroscopy, the transplanted BDOU with a grayish white surface were similar to the native common bile duct in morphology. On histology, the neo-bile duct was almost similar to the native common bile duct in morphology, and its portion possibly corresponding to bile duct epithelial cells was positive for cytokeratine19 just as the native bile duct was. Conclusion:The tubular BDOU of bioabsorbable polymer seeded with autologous bone marrow cells grew to form a shape similar to the native bile duct in 6 months after transplantation while functioning effectively as a bile duct. The results show that the artificial bile duct thus fabricated can be a substitute for the native bile duct. (LT) . Recurrent HCV is the leading cause of graft failure in these patients. It has recently been suggested that recurrent HCV may be more severe in patients who undergo living donor liver transplantation (LDLT) as opposed to transplantation with a graft from a deceased donor (DDLT). In our LT program, all patients with chronic HCV undergo prospective protocol liver biopsy (LBX) at periodic intervals to assess for fibrosis progression and do not receive interferon therapy until they reach Ishak stage 4 (early bridging fibrosis). This has allowed us to compare fibrosis progression associated with recurrent HCV in LDLT and DDLT. METHODS: Between 7/1998 and 12/2001 53 patients with well documented chronic HCV underwent DDLT and 23 LDLT. Primary immunosupression was similar in both groups and consisted of either cyclosporine, tacrolimus or sirulimus plus Cellcept and Prednisone (P). All patients remained on P for at least 6 months before this was discontinued. Both DDLT and LDLT patients underwent LBX 6, 12, 24 and 36 months following LT. Additional LBX were performed as needed to evaluate for abnormal liver chemistries. RESULTS: The LDLT and DDLT groups were well matched for age (47 yrs), race (77% Caucasian) and sex (82% male). Mean follow-up in both groups has now exceeded 3 years. CTP and MELD were significantly (p < 0.05) lower in LDLT vs DDLT. After 3 years graft survival was 76 and 89% in LDLT and DDLT (p=NS); patient survival was 79 and 91% (p=NS) respectively. The degree of fibrosis gradually worsened over time in both groups. However, no significant difference was observed between LDLT and DDLT groups. After 3 years mean fibrosis score was 0.9 and 1.9 in LDLT and DDLT groups respectively (p=NS). 50 and 47% of LDLT and DDLT recipients had developed portal fibrosis; 7 and 21% developed bridging fibrosis (p=NS). No pts had developed cirrhosis in either group. Acute rejection was observed in 14 and 21% of LDLT and DDLT (p=NS) . No significant difference in HCV RNA was observed in the two groups over time. CONCLUSION: Progressive fibrosis secondary to recurrent HCV does not occur more rapidly in patients who undergo LDLT compared to DDLT. Recurrent hepatitis C virus (HCV) is a serious problem post-transplant. Side effects of interferon-ribavirin combination therapy limit the sustained viral response achievable in a given HCV patient population. Coupled ribavirin/hemoglobin offers the potential of a therapy with improved safety and efficacy by targeting the delivery of ribavirin to key tissues (hepatocytes and macrophages) which possess receptors for binding and internalization of hemoglobin-haptoglobin complexes. This study was designed to evaluate the effect of a ribavirin-hemoglobin conjugate (RBV-Hb) in a mouse model of viral hepatitis induced by the coronavirus, MHV-3. Ribavirin was covalently coupled to highly purified hemoglobin HbAo at a mean molar drug ratio of 8:1 (ribavirin:hemoglobin). RBV-Hb was evaluated for retention of haptoglobin binding and cellular uptake in vitro, as well as the ability to recover bioactive ribavirin following enzymatic cleavage from RBV-Hb. The RBV-Hb-treated group was compared to control infected untreated and Ribavirin-treated groups for survival, clinical behavior, viral titer, biochemistry and liver histopathology. RBV-Hb was specifically internalized by cultured human and mouse hepatic cells but not by control non-hepatic cells. Ribavirin enzymatically cleaved from RBV-Hb retained in vitro bioactivity similar to unmodified ribavirin. Untreated and MHV-3 infected mice all developed clinical and biochemical signs of acute viral hepatitis and died by day 4 post infection (ALT max 7000 IU/L). Livers recovered from untreated and infected mice showed greater than 90% necrosis. In contrast, survival was enhanced in both Ribavirin and RBV-Hb treated and MHV-3 infected mice with a marked reduction in biochemical (ALT max 1000 IU/L) and histologic evidence of hepatic necrosis (<10%). Clinically, RBV-Hb treated mice behaved normally at all time points, in contrast to Ribavirin treated mice which developed lethargy and abnormal fur texture compatible with drug toxicity or ongoing viral infection. A beneficial effect of RBV-Hb was shown on the course of MHV-3 infection as demonstrated by prolonged survival, improved clinical behavior, and a reduction in biochemical and histologic disease. [Background] Development of de novo autoimmune hepatitis (de novo AIH) has been recently reported in patients with no evidence of autoimmune hepatitis before liver transplantation. Clinicopathological features of patients with de novo AIH have not been fully characterized. [Methods] Between 1990 and 2002, 671 patients who underwent liver transplantation in our Center survived more than one year. 79 patients developed chronic hepatitis (interface hepatitis with portal fibrosis). Among these 79, 19 patients fulfilled the criteria of definite or probable AIH according to the International AIH Group Criteria. Demographic data of HLA of both the recipients and the donors, all the episode biopsies after transplantation (n=103) were reviewed. [Results] The 19 patients (5 males and 14 females; the median age at transplantation was 9.4 years {0.7-26}) developed chronic hepatitis at a mean of 4.6 years (0.5 -10.5) after transplantation. Nine were definite AIH cases and 10 were probable cases. Autoantibodies and abnormally high level of serum IgG were found in 79% and 57% of the patients respectively. In most cases, basic immunosuppression was obtained with low-dose tacrolimus and no patients received azathioprine before development of de novo AIH. All were responsive to initial steroid therapy. Only 26% of the patients had HLA DR4, and none of them revealed HLA DR3. The most common findings before diagnosis of de novo AIH was acute cellular rejection (ACR), which was seen in 68% of the patients (control group; 49%). ACR more than 6 months after transplantation (late ACR) was seen more frequently than control group (42% vs. 17%, p<0.01). The interval between the diagnosis of de novo AIH and the last late ACR was 2.4 +/-1.5 years (mean +/-S.D.). Centrilobular fibrosis (CF) was demonstrated in all but one case (95%) at the diagnosis of de novo AIH, and 63% of the patients showed CF at the median of 1.1 years (1.5 months -4.9 years) after transplantation. [Conclusion] Female predominance and steroid responsiveness were noted in de novo AIH patients, similarly to conventional AIH, but lower frequency of HLA DR3/DR4 and preceding CF were unique findings seen in de novo AIH. Histological features such as late ACR and CF may be risk factors for the development of de novo AIH. Purpose. We previously reported the incidence of myocardial injury during liver transplantation and the poor correlation of this event with the results of preoperative dobutamine stress echocardiography. We have now investigated the association between specific preoperative cardiovascular risk factors, intraoperative hemodynamic instability and intraoperative myocardial injury in the same population. Methods. 117 adult patients undergoing orthotopic liver transplantation were studied. Preoperative risk factors noted included age, gender, diabetes, hypertension, smoking, obesity, arrhythmia and known coronary artery disease. The prevalence of intraoperative hemodynamic instability was noted. Hemodynamic instability was defined as the occurrence of either significant arterial hypotension (systolic pressure <80mmHg for >10 consecutive minutes), use of a vasopressor infusion, cardiac arrest or significant arrhythmia. Troponin was measured postoperatively in all patients to assess myocardial injury. Statistical comparisons were by Fisher's exact test or Student's t-test as appropriate. Results and conclusion. Postoperative troponin elevation occurred in 14 patients (12%). Hemodynamic instability occurred in 24 patients. 9/14 patients with elevated postoperative troponin displayed hemodynamic instability (4 hypotension, 2 vasopressor infusion, 2 cardiac arrest, 1 new arrhythmia) compared to 15/103 patients who did not have troponin elevation (14 hypotension, 1 vasopressor infusion). This difference was statistically significant, p<0.001. No pre-operative risk factor was significantly associated with postoperative troponin elevation. We conclude that myocardial damage during liver transplantation is associated with intraoperative hemodynamic changes; no preoperative risk factor we assessed identified those at risk. Methods. Vasotrac radial artery pressure measurements were compared with simultaneous invasive radial artery pressures (contralateral arm) in 14 patients undergoing liver transplantation. Correlation between the two methods was calculated and a Bland-Altman analysis performed to assess bias and limits of agreement (± 2sd). Results and Conclusions. 6468 simultaneous measurements were made over a duration of 1.5-7.5hrs per case. The study was terminated prior to the end of the case in 2 patients, one secondary to a cool finger distal to the device, the other to significant pressure markings. No adverse sequelae were noted. Vasotrac recordings became unobtainable for some period during 2 cases. For mean arterial pressure 57% of Vasotrac measurements were within 10% of direct arterial measurement. Correlation was 0.81. Vasotrac bias was +5.9mmHg, limits of agreement were ±19.6mmHg. For systolic arterial pressure 65% of Vasotrac measurements were withing 10% of direct arterial measurement. Correlation was 0.78. Vasotrac bias was +7.6mmHg, limits of agreement ±25mmHg. For diastolic arterial pressure 57% of Vasotrac measurements were within 10% of direct arterial measurement. Correlation was 0.81. Vasotrac bias was +3.3mmHg, limits of agreement ±15mmHg. We conclude that the Vasotrac is not adequately accurate to substitute for direct pressure monitoring during liver transplantation. Transplantation of liver with severe steatosis may result in primary nonfunction (PNF) of the graft. To date, protective strategy is still rare. As previously decribed by our group, human recombinant hepatocyte growth factor (hrHGF) has a cytoprotective effect on fatty liver during cold preservation in rats. Here we further examined the effects of hrHGF on survival and graft function in rat fatty liver transplantation. Methods Severe steatosis was induced by a 15-day choline-deficient diet in male Lewis rats. The fatty graft was orthotopically transplanted in normal male Lewis rat after 12 hr cold preservation in UW solution. In the HGF group, donor rats were pretreated with a single bolus of systemic injection of hrHGF (100µg/rat), and 30µg of hrHGF was also added to the perfusion/preservation solution. Animal survival, graft function and morphological changes were compared with those of the untreated group. Liver cell apoptosis was detected by TUNEL stain and liver regeneration was evaluated by BrdU stain at 2 hrs after reperfusion of the liver graft. Results One-week survival was significantly improved in the HGF group compared with the untreated group (83% vs. 33%, p<0.01, n=18/group). AST, ALT and LDH levels in the graft rinse effluent at the end of cold preservation and serum ALT and LDH at 2 hrs after reperfusion were significantly low in the HGF group. Although there was no significant difference in hyaluronic acid (HA) level in the rinse effluents at the end of preservation between the two groups, exogenous HA uptake rate (HAUR) at 2 hrs after reperfusion was improved in the HGF group (0.62±0.05 vs. 0.79±0.11, p<0.05). Morphologically, sinusoidal endothelial cells (SECs) and hepatocytes were well preserved as observed by transmission electron microscope in the HGF group compared with the control. hrHGF pretreatment also significantly reduced the (SEC) apoptotic index (1.65±0.60% vs. 4.90±1.25%, p<0.01) and increased BrdU incorporation index ( 2.07±0.62% vs. 0.06±0.04%, p<0.01) at 2 hrs after reperfusion. Conclusions hrHGF prevented the death of PNF in steatotic liver transplantation by attenuating preservation-reperfusion injury and facilitating recovery of the graft, probably through its antiapoptotic properties and acceleration of liver regeneration at early stage after transplantation. Operational tolerance {graft acceptance in an immunosuppression (IS) -free environment} after LDLT occurs in a substantial number (approximately 40%) of the patients by our elective protocol 72,449,2001) . There is, nevertheless, no reliable parameter to monitor and select patients who may discontinue IS without a risk of rejection. To identify such parameters, we analyzed systemically liver transplant (Tx) patients exhibiting operational tolerance for the profiles of peripheral blood lymphocytes. Methods. Seventeen liver Tx patients (Gr-tol) regarded as tolerant were enrolled in this study. FACS analysis was performed to determine the phenotype of peripheral blood lymphocytes. As controls, twenty-four age-matched volunteers with normal liver function (Gr-vol) were analyzed. Results. An increase was observed in the percentages of B cells, CD8 + T cells, CD4 + CD25 high+ cells and Vd1gdT cells in peripheral blood lymphocytes from Gr-tol, compared with those from Gr-vol (p<0.01, p=0.14, p<0.05, p<0.01, respectively). On the other hand, the percentages of CD4 + T cells, NK cells, NKT cells, and V2gdT cells were decreased in Gr-tol, compared with those in p<0.05, p<0.05, p<0.01, respectively) . There was no significant difference between the two groups with respect to the percentages of pan-T cells, total CD4 + CD25 + cells and ab / gd T cells (NS, respectively) . The similar tendency was found in the absolute number of each lymphocyte phenotype. Conclusion. Present results revealed several intriguing features of peripheral blood lymphocyte subsets in patients exhibiting operational tolerance after LDLT. Although the contribution of those subsets to the tolerant state remains elusive, the results may provide clues for reliable indicators of tolerance after human liver Tx. Purpose: Ischemia/reperfusion injury (IRI) to the liver occurs frequently during hepatectomies and is unavoidable during liver transplantation. The goal of this study was to determine whether mild and clinically acceptable hypothermia could provide a protective effect against IRI in a warm ischemia rat model. Methods: Male lean Zucker rats (Weight: 303 ± 31 grams, Age: 10-13 weeks old) were subject to 75 minutes of warm ischemia with reperfusion of the left and median lobes (70% partial ischemia) under isoflurane anesthesia. Control rats were carefully maintained with a body temperature of 37.1 ± 0.14 ° Cusing heat lamps and warming pads. Experimental rats were exposed to ambient temperature and allowed to cool spontaneously to a core temperature of 33.4 ± 0.21 °C just before reperfusion of the liver. Survival rates were compared after 24 hours of reperfusion and blood and liver tissue of surviving animals were harvested for histological and biochemical analysis. Results: All mildly hypothermic rats survived in good general condition (n=5). In contrast, 1 of 6 control rat died within 24 hours and rats that survived were poor health condition. Histological analysis of liver tissue revealed significant necrosis in the control rats while hypothermic rats exhibited only mild histological changes. Serum AST and ALT levels at 24 hours were significantly higher in the control rats compared to the experimental group (AST: 15,113 ± 4,336 vs. 1,234 ± 980, ALT: 5,884 ± 2,063 vs. 714 ± 765, all p<0.01). Conclusion: Mild hypothermia achieved by exposure to ambient temperatures during surgery was sufficient to provide significant protection against IRI in a clinically relevant in vivo rat model. The efficacy of Highly Active Anti-Retroviral Treatment (HAART) leads to consider LT in HIV patients. We evaluated 10 HIV-positive patients transplanted during the last 4 years. The chart of 10 HIV patients aged from 34 to 49 years transplanted from 2000 was collected in two centres. Chronic HCV and HBV co-infection was present in 2; chronic HCV in 5 and chronic HBV in one. Nine patients received HAART before transplantation. Patients were transplanted for fulminant hepatic failure due to HAART toxicity in 2 and for chronic end stage liver disease in 8.Cadaveric graft was transplanted in 9 and right liver from living donor in one. CD4 + cell count was > 200 cells/mm 3 in 8 and HIV viral load was undetectable in 7 patients. Antirejection immunosuppression was begun within the first 24 h after transplantation with tacrolimus (FK506) and one or more of the following: corticosteroids, mycophenolate mofetil and azatiopirine. HAART treatment was restored within the first 40 days after OLT in 4 patients. Postoperative mortality (within 60 days) was 10% and 8 are alive to date (from 6 to 47 months). Postoperative surgical complications, including hepatic artery thrombosis in 2, were present in 5 (50%). Among the four patients who received HAART after LT, HAART liver toxicity was observed in two HCV co-infected patients and 2 experienced Tacrolimus toxicity related to protease inhibitors. In the patients with protease inhibitors Tacrolimus blood levels between 5 and 10µg/ L were achieved with doses of 0,5 mg every 3 days. In the 2 patients who permanently received HAART after LT, HIV viral load remained undetectable but postoperative CD4 + cell count was always < 200mm 3 . In 6 of the 7 patients in which HAART treatment was not restored or discontinued after LT, CD4 + cell count remained > 200/mm 3 and HIV viral load remained undetectable in only one. Our experience confirms that LT is effective in selected HIV patients with excellent results in those without co-infection C. The survival of patients who do not received post transplantation HAART treatment seems not to be compromised. Abhinav Humar 1 , Kristin Horn 1 , Ann Kalis 1 , Brooke Glessing 1 , Angelika C. Lake 2 . 1 Surgery, University of Minnesota, Minneapolis, MN, USA; 2 Medicine, University of Minnesota, Minneapolis, MN, USA Background: Late renal dysfunction and eventual renal failure is an increasing concern and problem in liver transplant recipients. Long-term CNI use is believed to play a contributing role in many of these cases. There is increasing interest in CNI withdrawal from maintenance regimens in an attempt to improve renal function. We instituted a protocol of CNI withdrawal in liver transplant recipients with documented deterioration of their renal function. Patients who were at least 6 months posttransplant, with a rising serum Cr (a 50% inrease from their baseline value or a serum Cr consistently greater than 2 mg/dl), and no recent (within 3 months) acute rejection (AR) episode were eligible. These patients were converted from a CNI maintenance protocol to a regimen consisting of full-dose MMF (1g bid) and low-dose prednisone (5 mg/day). Recipients that were off prednisone, were restarted on it at the time of the conversion. Results: Since Jan 99, we have converted 13 recipients using this protocol. Mean recipient age was 57.3 yrs. Causes of liver failure were Hep C (3), PBC (2) , Hep B (1), ETOH (4) , and others (3). Mean time posttransplant at time of switch was 46.1 months (range=6-108 months). Six of the recipients were on CsA; 7 were on tacrolimus. Mean serum Cr at time of switch was 2.4 mg/dl (range=1. 6-5.9 ). Mean estimated Cr clearance was 37.9 mls/min (range=18-63). With a mean follow up of 22.9 months (range 4-58) since CNI withdrawal, the following results are seen: i) None of the recipients have had an episode of AR after the switch ii) 12 recipients remain on MMF/Pred; 1 has restarted on FK with the MMF being stopped because of a very low WBC count. iii) No recipient has progressed to end-stage renal disease and dialysis iv) 11 of the 13 recipients have had an improvement in renal function, and 2 have had no change. No recipient has had a deterioration of renal function. v) Mean serum Cr for all recipients at present is 1.7 (range=1.0-3.0) (p=0.02 vs. pre switch); estimated Cr clearance= 51.8 (range= 19-86) (p=0.02). Conclusions: CNI withdrawal with conversion to MMF/Pred is a viable option in LTx recipients with deteriorating renal function. There did not seem to be a significant increase in the risk for AR after switching in this small group of patients. Renal function was noted to improve in the majority of recipients after stopping the CNI. Roberto Troisi 1 , Lucien Noens 2 , Bart Vandekerckhove 3 , Roberto Montalti 1 , Salvatore Ricciardi 1 , Jan Philippe 4 , Bernard de Hemptinne 1 . 1 Dept. of General, Hepato-Biliary and Transplantation Surgery; 2 Hematology; 3 Immunohematology Lab-Blood Bank; 4 Clinical Biology, Ghent University Hospital Medical School, Ghent-Belgium Introduction. Graft and patient survival rates in recipients of AB0 incompatible (AB0-I) liver transplantation is poor due to the greater incidence of vascular thrombosis, biliary injury and acute cellular rejection (ACR). AB0 mismatch liver transplantation is controversial especially in non-urgent situations and in living donor liver transplantation. We investigate a new protocol consisting on highly selective apheresis (HSA) using Glycosorb® AB device that remove only blood group antibodies, associated with daclizumab and mycophenolate mofetil induction without splenectomy in living donor liver transplantation in adults (AALDLT). Methods. Between January and April 2003, 2 out of 10 recipients underwent AB0-I (A 1 >0; A 2 >B) AALDLT with the right lobe in our institution. Case 1 was a man 45 y.o. and case 2 was a man 52 y.o., underwent transplantation for multifocal HCC on HCV-related cirrhosis both. HSA was performed on preoperative day -2 and -1 and following AALDLT according to the anti-IgM/IgG titer increase. Immunosuppressive regimen consisted on daclizumab (1mg/Kg) induction on day -1; 1; 15; 30; 45; mycophenolate mofetil 2 x 1 g on day -1; 0 and each postoperative day; tacrolimus 2 x 1 mg on day -1 and each postoperative day with doses adjusted to maintain serum through levels of 12-15 ng/dl. Conventional steroid tapering doses was administered. Results. After a median FU of 9 months, both patients are alive and well. In patient 1, preoperative anti-IgM/IgG titer before and after HSA fall from 128 to 4 and from 64 to 2 respectively. No ACR episodes occurred. Anti IgG titers remained between 1 and 8. In patient 2, preoperative anti IgM/IgG titers fall from 1 to 0 and from 32 to 0. This patient experienced two episodes of ACR grade 2 on the third postoperative week and at 6 months. Anti IgG titers remained between 2 and 16. A standard plasmapheresis was performed at 6 months due to the increase of Anti IgM and IgG titers. Both patients did not experienced major complications or infections episodes or CMV disease. Conclusions. AALDLT from AB0 incompatible donors has been successfully performed using this new approach. Selective apheresis with Glycosorb AB® has showed high efficiency to decrease significantly anti blood group antibodies. This treatment may potentially expand the donor pool in AALDLT with minimal morbidity and good outcome. IN CHINESE LIVER TRANSPLANT RECIPIENTS TREATED Objective To clinically validate basiliximab is an effective induction therapy antibody in Chinese liver transplant recipients. Methods 48 patients with cirrhotic liver disease, fulminant hepatitis or HCC, aged from 26 to 60 years old, and 37 male, 11 female, who all received liver transplant from cadaveric donor, were double-blinded and randomly divided into two groups. Patients were administered 40 mg of basiliximab (n = 24) or placebo (n = 24) as two 20-mg bolus injections days 0 and 4, plus cyclosporine and steroids. The first dose should be given within 2 hours after organ perfusion and the second on day 4 after transplantation. In addition, all patients were administered triple immunosuppressive therapy with cyclosporine(Neoral), steroids and mycophenolate mofetil (MMF). The efficacy of basiliximab was assessed by conventional parameters, and tolerability was assessed by the incidence of adverse events, infections, and laboratory test result abnormalities. All cases who were doubtful of acute rejection in clinic received biopsy-confirmed acute rejection. No one required re-transplantation for severe complication. Results At 3 months, the first acute biopsy-confirmed rejection episodes were more frequent in placebo group 37.5% (9/24) than basiliximab group 12.5% (3/24) (p=0.048). The incidence of infection consisting of lung , blood, and ascites between two groups were 52.6% and 50.0%, respectively. Cyclosporin (5-8 mg/kg/day) was started at or after 72 h (n=13 ) in basiliximab group , and Cyclosporin (5-10 mg/kg/day) in placebo group. Nephrotoxicity were seen in the basiliximab group and placebo group (10.5% and 20.0%, respectively). The refractory rejection episode was only happened in placebo group(n=1). Basiliximab caused no injection-site reactions, anaphylaxis, or cytokine release syndrome. Conclusion Immunoprophylaxis with 40 mg of basiliximab in combination with triple immunosuppressive therapy provides increased efficacy in reducing the incidence of acute rejection episodes, with no clinically significant increase in adverse events. Use of basiliximab combined with triple immunosuppressive therapy can use low-dose Cyclosporin, meanwhile, reduced incidence of nephrotoxicity. Objective To evaluate the safety and reliability of Neoral C 2 monitoring, and determine the target level of C 2 in Chinese adult liver transplant recipients. Methods 31 de novo Chinese adult liver transplant recipients were randomized to three groups( C 0 group, "high" C 2 group, "low" C 2 group) according to different monitoring tools and target levels. During follow-up, at each visit, the physical exam was performed and routine blood chemistry was obtained including CsA levels.The change of immune status and episodes of rejection were also observed closely. Results The "low" C 2 group had the lowest Neoral oral dose(2.40±0.26mg/kg/d), and had remarkable difference compared with the other groups (P<0.001). The best liver,heart and renal function was observed in "low" C 2 group. The CD4 + / CD8 + of "low" C 2 group was 1.10±0.46, has no remarkable difference with C 0 group. The rejection incidence of three groups were very similar. "low" C 2 group had highest clinical benefit(72.73%), while the clinical benefit of "high" C 2 group is the lowest(9.09%). Conclusions With rational target levels, C 2 monitoring can decrease the oral dose of Neoral and side effects remarkablely without the increase of rejection incidence.The target level of C 2 in Chinese adult liver transplant recipients is: 600-800ng/ ml during 1 to 6 months after transplantation, 400-600 ng/ml from 6 to 12 months, 200-400 ng/ml after 12 months. Tacrolimus has been increasingly used in liver transplant patients to prevent rejection. The drug has relatively a narrow therapeutic window. We report here the changes in the kinetic profile following oral dose of tacrolimus, before and after reversal of Jejuno-ileal (J-I) bypass. Case 1: 57 year old lady underwent deceased liver transplantation (LTx) in February, 2003 for end stage liver disease related to (J-I) bypass, 20 years prior to LTx for obesity. Her stable dose of tacrolimus was 7mg twice a day, with trough concentration ranging from 5.2 to 6.4 ng/ml. She underwent reversal of (J-I) bypass seven months post LTx. Kinetic studies were performed prior to reversal of (J-I) bypass and 3 months after reversal with the stable oral dose of tacrolimus in steady state. Results: Immediately after reversal of (J-I) bypass her trough level increased to 25.4 ng/ ml from 6.2 ng/ml. Tacrolimus was put on hold for 2 days and then restarted at 3mg twice a day. Subsequently she stabilized to 5 mg twice a day to achieve trough concentration ranging from 4.1 to 6.6 ng/ml. The area under the concentration curve (AUC) improved from 10.9 to 20.7 ng/ml/hr/mg, a two-fold increase with J-I reversal. Also peak value of twice the trough concentration was observed at three hours after the oral dose of tacrolimus, following reversal of I-J bypass, which was absent prior to reversal (figure below). Conclusion: Prior to reversal of J-I Bypass patients required higher doses of tacrolimus and kinetic study did no show any peak value. After reversal of J-I bypass patient demonstrated regular peak value and required lower doses. Also AUC after J-I reversal improved by two fold. In the immediate postoperative period, toxic levels were observed probably because of large area of absorption with relative adynamic bowel activity. Careful monitoring is necessary in such situation to avoid tacrolimus related drug toxicity. Background/Aim: Immunoprophylaxis using intravenous (IV) hepatitis B immunoglobulin (HBIG) decreases the recurrence of hepatitis B virus (HBV) infection after orthotopic liver transplantation (OLT). However, IV HBIG is expensive, has significant side effects. The efficacy of an alternative prophylaxis using intramuscular (IM) HBIG and lamivudine was prospectively analyzed in this study. Materials and Methods: The study population included 66 HBsAg-positive patients (55 males, 11 female; mean age 47 years; 35 HBV DNA-positive, 33 HBeAg-positive) who underwent OLT between Jan 1999 and Dec 2002. The patients with HBV replicative status (HBV DNA-positive, or HBeAg-positive) was 74% (49/66). Four patients (3 patients had YMDD mutation) were treated with oral lamivudine over one year before OLT. All patients received 10,000 IU of IV HBIG monthly and lamivudine daily during first 6 months after OLT and then switched to 2000 IU of IM HBIG bimonthly and oral lamivudine 100 mg daily. Recurrent HBV was defined as the reappearance of hepatitis B surface antigen. Quantitative hepatitis B surface antibody (HBsAb) was measured monthly using Abbott Laboratory RIA AUSAB kit. The quantitation of HBV DNA was measured by bDNA method (detection limit: 10 5 copies/ ml) Results: During a median follow up of 25.9 months (duration 12-60 months), only one (1.5%) of 66 patients showed the recurrence of HBV 11 months after OLT. The mean titer of HBsAb (S/N) at 6 month, 12 month, 18 month, 24 month after OLT were 96.3±5. 3, 84.4±7.4, 96.7±11.3, 88.1±18.4, respectively. Three patients with YMDD mutation before OLT had no recurrence of HBV after combination prophylaxis (60, 24, 23 months after OLT, respectively). Conclusions: Combination prophylaxis with IM HBIG and lamivudine is highly effective in preventing recurrent HBV regardless of HBV replication status, may protect against the emergence of resistant mutants, and is cost-effective strategy than HBIG mono-therapy. This combination may be one of effective prophylaxis against patients with YMDD mutation before OLT. The controversy remains whether such patients should receive further transplantation for recurrence of disease. Steve Woodle, Steven M. Rudich. Transplant Surgery, University of Cincinnati, Cincinnati, OH, USA INTRODUCTION: Cirrhosis due to hepatitis C virus (HCV) is a leading indication for orthotopic liver transplantation (OLT). Following OLT for HCV, as many as 80 to 90% of all allografts become re-infected. As many transplant programs are utilizing extended criteria donor (ECD) livers, the impact of this practice on recurrent HCV infection remains unknown. PURPOSE: To analyze rates of HCV re-infection in OLT recipients receiving ECD livers compared to a control group of standard criteria donor (SCD) liver allografts. METHODS: All OLTs performed between Jan 2002-Oct 2003 were categorized as having received either an ECD or SCD liver at our center. ECD grafts were defined as having any one or more of the following: donor age > 60 yrs, biopsy exhibiting >40% macrovesicular and/or >75% microvesicular steatosis, any donor down time (±CPR), requirement of 2 or more pressors within 12 hours of procurement, or liver function studies > 5 x upper limit of normal. All recipients underwent liver biopsy at 90 days or earlier, if there was an appreciable rise in baseline liver function tests (LFTs). RESULTS: The two groups (ECD and SCD liver recipients) were similar in regards to: MELD score (match and lab), gender, age, and graft ischemic (total and warm) times. Ninety OLTs were performed during the study period: 42 ECD livers and 48 SCD livers were transplanted. HCV-related cirrhosis accounted for 24/42 (57.1%) of the OLTs in the ECD group and 20/48 (41.7%) of the OLTs in the SCD group (p=0.14). Biopsy-proven recurrent HCV infection was noted in 21 (23.3%) of all allografts. The ECD group was found to have a 33.3% recurrence rate (8/24) while in the SCD group a recurrence rate of 65% (13/48) was noted (p=0.04). No significant differences were observed between the groups in LTFs of patients requiring biopsies. CONCLUSION: The aggressive use of extended criteria donor hepatic allografts was noted not to lead to an increased rate of HCV re-infection in the recipient allograft. Indeed, there was a statistically significant increase in HCV re-infection in those recipients receiving standard criteria donor livers. This suggests that perhaps a "stressed donor allograft" is protected from viral re-infection. The long term outcome following transplantation for HCV has deteriorated questioning whether use of more potent immunosuppressant agents contributes to lower patient and graft survival. This study was designed to evaluate outcome differences in patients receiving Neoral C2 as compared to tacrolimus C0 following liver transplantation for HCV. 128 patients underwent liver transplantation for HCV at our center; 48 received Neoral and 80 Tacrolimus. All received Solumedrol/prednisone. C2 levels of Neoral were adjusted to 1000 ng/mL for the first 6 months and then lowered to 800 ng/mL months 6-12 and then 600 ng/mL thereafter whereas tacrolimus levels were kept between 10-15 ng/ml for months 1-6 and then reduced to 5-10 ng/mL after month 6. Rates and severity of rejection were similar for patients receiving Neoral or Tacrolimus ((37% vs 34%, p=NS). Patient and graft survival was higher in patients receiving Neoral compared to those receiving Tacrolimus (90% vs 78% p<0.05). The incidence and severity of recurrent HCV was much higher in patients receiving Tacrolimus with many patients progressing to severe fibrosis or cirrhosis by year 3 post transplant. HCV viral titers increased more rapidly in patients treated with Tacrolimus than Neoral and by 3 years mean viral titers were 3.2 million copies in patients receiving Tacrolimus versus 1.05 million copies in patients treated with Neoral (p<0.05). Levels of AST, ALT, ALP and bilirubin were more markedly disturbed in patients receiving Tacrolimus. Use of rebetron only provided a sustained virologic response in patients on Neoral as previously reported, although the sustained virologic response was only seen in 20% of treated patients. The incidence of diabetes mellitus was significantly higher in patients treated with Tacrolimus. Addition of Cellcept (MMF) resulted in more severe recurrent HCV infection in both Neoral and Tacrolimus treated groups. These data strongly suggest that use of Tacrolimus in comparison to Neoral leads to a poorer outcome following transplantation for HCV. Multivariate analysis of this single center data as well as analysis of the Lis2t study will hopefully provide further insight into factors contributing to these results which will allow us to design better immunosuppressive protocols for this group of patients. Along with the evolution of adult living donor liver transplantation (LDLTx) the number of transplants for hepatitis C (HepC) cirrhosis has been increasing with LDLTx worldwide. The results are reported to be unsatisfactory compared to the transplantation for other indications due to recurrence of HepC after transplantation. We analyzed the outcome of LDLTx for HepC in our institute in terms of the factors affecting the HepC recurrence and the efficacy of treatment for recurrence. Data were collected by retrospective chart review and were analyzed with Kaplan-Meier method and Cox proportional hazard regression method. Immunosuppression after transplantation consists of tacrolimus and low dose steroids. Preoperative or posttransplant preemptive viral therapy was not done. Posttransplant liver biopsy was done for abnormal liver function tests. From March 1999 till 2003, 72 patients (male 50, female 22) received LDLTx for HepC cirrhosis. Mean age was 54 years old (33∼69 years old). Blood group combination was identical with 48 cases, compatible with 13 cases and incompatible with 11 cases. Genome typing showed 1b with 35 cases. Preoperative viral load (KIU/ml) was 260 (mean), varying from <0.5 to 1440. Kaplan-Meier analysis showed almost same patient survival with HepC cirrhosis and with other indications (77% at 4 years). HepC cirrhosis complicated by hepatocellular carcinoma showed lower patient survival compared to HepC cirrhosis alone. Cumulative virological recurrence rate was 94% with histological recurrence rate of 64% (mean follow-up period 23.2 months). Factors affecting posttranspalnt HepC recurrence include recipient age (older), recipient sex (female), donor sex (male) and donor body mass index (higher). For the HepC recurrence (histological), 24 patients underwent interferon + ribavirin therapy. At the time of diagnosis, 30% of cases showed moderate fibrosis (F2). HCV-RNA became negative after treatment in 8 out of 18 recipients. Conclusions: > Intra-institutional comparison showed no significant difference in terms of recipient survival either with HepC or other indications. > Virological recurrence is unavoidable. > Protocol biopsy for monitoring recurrence may be effective for prompt treatment. Neuhaus 1 . 1 Surgery, Charite Virchow Clinic Humboldt University, Berlin, Germany Background: The optimal immunosuppressive regimen for HCV positive liver transplant recipients has not been established. Treatment for acute rejection wiht steroids is associated to increased viral replication and graft hepatitis.We retrospectively analyzed 232 patients after orthotopic liver transplantation (OLT) for HCV cirrhosis to determine the influence of methylprednisolon pulse therapy on long term outcome after OLT. Methods: 232 liver transplants in HCV recepients between 1989 and 2001 were analyzed. Median follow-up was 4.4 years, median age of patients was 53 years (15-72y). Immunsuppression consisted of Tacrolimus (Tac) or Cyclosporine A (CyA) in different protocols. All rejection episodes were histologically proven. Results: 28/232 graft losses due to severe hepatitis C reinfection occured (12.06%). 105/ 232 patients showed a minimum of one episode of acute rejection (45.25%). 71/86 patients received methylprednisolon pulse therapy for one time (82.55%). 15/86 required treatment with OKT3 for steroid-resistant rejection (17.44%). 19/232 patients required repeated steroid pulse therapy due to more than one episode of acute rejection (8.1.%). In patients with more than one episode of acute rejection the risk of HCV related graft loss was significantly enhanced (6/19, p<0.017). The primary immunosuppresion had no influence of the outcome in our data. Conclusion: Our data show that outcome of HCV positive patients with repeated steroid pulse therapy for acute rejection is significant worse compared to patients with single pulse therapy. Therefore repeated steroid pulse therapies should be avoided in HCV positive transplant recipients. New strategies in managing acute rejection in these patients are required. Invasive Aspergillosis (IA) has reported to occur in 1 to 8% of liver transplant recipients and less frequently kidney recipients. The mortality related to IA is significantly high and early diagnosis remains a challenge. Several studies have indicated a promising role for circulating markers of infection, using a double-sandwich (ds) ELISA (Platelia Aspergillus, Bio-Rad) that detects galactomannan (GM). In prolonged neutropenic patients and stem cell transplantation recipients, GM detection has proved to be an effective diagnostic tool with an excellent sensitivity; its usefulness is debatable in solid organ transplant setting. Methods: from December 1999 to December 2003 a total of 296 Elisa tests were performed in 92 high risk patients submitted to liver (64) or kidney (28) transplant (3.2 tests/pt) in order to evaluate the diagnostic potential of circulating galactomannan. Risk factors for IA were considered: poor/late graft function; renal failure/HD; Cytomegalovirus reactivation; nadir PLT count below 30.000/ml; greater transfusion requirements; retransplantation. GM detection was performed by a double-sandwich Elisa test and was considered positive if optical density was found to be higher than 1.2.Results: Twenty-three out of 92 patients had a positive Elisa test at least once, that was instead consistently negative in 69 out of 92 patients. According to CDC diagnostic criteria for IA, no cases of "defined" or "probable" IA occurred in the study population; in 5 patients diagnosis of "possible" IA was done. As a tool for "defined" or "probable" IA, GM detection yields a specificity of 75% (25% of false positive results). As a screening test for "possible" IA, specificity increases to 78% with a sensitivity of 62%; in our population with a low incidence of IA, the positive predictive value of the test was 22%, while the negative predictive value was 95%.Conclusion: According to our experience, GM detection usefulness as screening test for early diagnosis of invasive aspergillosis is largely debatable; due to the high number of false-positive results, "defined" as well as "probable" IA diagnosis can not rely on GM detection and it seems reasonable that alternative circulating antigens might be identified for the early diagnosis of IA. Cytomegalovirus is the main pathogen of liver transplantation (LT) patients. During immunossuppression, the patients require rigorous clinical and laboratory monitoring to prevent or treat opportunist agents such as CMV and HHV-6. Rapid tests such as antigenemia and nested PCR are currently used for this purpose. Objective: The objective of this study was to monitor Liver Transplant patients for active CMV and HHV-6 infection after transplantation. Methods: Seventeen patients were monitored. The follow-up to detect active infection for these virus consisted of antigenemia (AGM) for CMV and PCR for CMV and HHV-6 , from before the transplant and up to 365 days post-transplant. If an active infection was detected, gancyclovir was initiated followed by maintenance therapy. Results: Fiveteen patients (88.2%) had two or more positive CMV-PCR results, six patients (35.3%) had at least one cell positive for CMV-antigenemia. Among the patients with an active CMV infection , eleven (64.7%) had probably CMV-disease. Positive PCR results were obteined in median 77 days before antigenemia and in median 37 days after transplantation. Twelve of seventeen patients were folowed-up for HHV-6 using PCR technique. Among these 12 patients, 91,6% had positive HCMV PCR and 66,7% had positive HHV-6 PCR. The CMV/HHV-6 co-infection rate was 41,6% . Four patients had acute graft rejection episodes. All of them had positive CMV PCR and 75% had positive HHV-6 PCR. Conclusion: We conclude that antigenemia and PCR techniques are efficient in the followup of active CMV infections. Background: Long-term neurologic recovery and complications in children receiving a liver transplant for FHF are not well described. The purpose of this analysis was to determine if there were long-term neurologic sequelae once these patients had recovered from their liver transplant. We also tried to determine the impact of this on long-term schooling in these children. Results: We studied outcomes in pediatric patients receiving a successful liver transplant for FHF. Only patients transplanted after 1990 were included in this analysis. Excluded were recipients who had early graft loss or died within the first 3 months after transplant. A total of 10 recipients were identified that fit the study criteria. Mean age at time of transplant was 8.2 years (range = 1-18). There were no documented neurologic abnormalities in these patients prior to the onset of FHF. Cause of FHF were Hep A (n=1), autoimmune (n=1), acetaminophen (n=1), and unknown (n=7). Seven of the 10 recipients had severe encephalopathy (Grade III or IV) at the time of transplant. Mean waiting time from presentation to transplant was 8.8 days (range = 2-23 days). With a mean follow up of 6.3 years (range 1-12 years), all 10 recipients are alive with functioning grafts. Neurologic complications in the 10 recipients include persistent seizures in 2 of the recipients -in 1 recipient the seizures are well controlled medically , but the other recipient has ongoing severe seizure disorder and is now globally aphasic as a result of these. Of the other 8, 1 has major developmental and language delays and another 2 recipient have mild cognitive impairment on formal neuropsychological testing. Another 3 recipients have had major behavior issues requiring psychological intervention. With regards to schooling 5 are at an age appropriate grade level, 2 are 2 grade levels behind, 1 is 1 grade level behind, 1 does not attend school due to neurologic impairment, and another left school after 2 years due to behavioral problems. Conclusions: Long-term major and minor neurologic problems are not uncommon in pediatric patients transplanted for FHF. Schooling is commonly impacted upon in these patients. These problems should be anticipated and adequately addressed in the long-term management of these patients after recovery from their transplant. We encountered two cases of pediatric living-related liver transplant recipients who showed the increases in blood concentration of tacrolimus (Tac) or cyclosporine (CsA) during diarrheal episode. First case was a 2.3 year-old girl. Tac was orally given at a dose of 1.8 mg twice daily (0.34 mg/kg/day) and the trough blood concentrations were stable (9.7 -10.3 ng/mL) until 29 posttransplant day (POD). On POD 32, she had diarrhea caused by rotavirus infection, and the concentration elevated to 18.0 ng/mL on POD 35. The second case was a 1.6 year-old boy. CsA (Neoral®) was orally given at a dose of 80 mg twice daily (14 mg/kg/day). The blood trough concentration of CsA was 161 to 230 ng/mL. On POD 15, he had fever and watery diarrhea, and the concentration elevated to 571 ng/ mL on POD 17. Tac and CsA are well-known dual substrates for cytochrome P-450 (CYP) 3A and P-glycoprotein (P-gp). Both CYP3A and P-gp exist in the enterocytes of human small intestine. Inflammation and infection in the intestine potentially increase the bioavailability. To investigate the effect of intestinal inflammation on the metabolic and efflux pump activities, we conducted experiments using the lipopolysaccharide (LPS)induced intestinal damage model in rats. Intestinal epithelial CYP3A activity was assessed by nifedipine oxidation using rat intestinal epithelial microsomes. Drug efflux by P-gp was tested by digoxin flux in excised rat intestine perfusion system. LPS (0.3 mg/kg) significantly reduced the intestinal epithelial CYP3A activity by 41 % (P<0.01). In the proximal jejunal segment in the rats treated with LPS, mucosal to serosal flux of digoxin was significantly enhanced compared to that of control (+91 % at 120 min perfusion, P<0.05). Efflux of digoxin, which was uptaken to intestinal epithelium, to mucosal perfusate was significantly blunted in the jejunum treated with LPS (-48 % at 120 min, P<0.05), which indicates that digoxin efflux into intestinal lumen is decreased in the small intestine treated with LPS. These results suggest that reduced intestinal CYP3A and P-gp function may partly be involved in the mechanism of the enteritis-related increase in the blood concentration of Tac and CsA. HBO therapy helps to oxygenate ischemic hepatocytes by increasing the oxygen carrying capacity of hemoglobin and increase in soluble oxygen concentration in the blood. It also helps to reduce edema by down regulating adhesion molecules leading to diminished leukocyte and platelet adhesion and capillary injury. The current status of this treatment modality is discussed. Using appropriate support, i.e., continuous hemodiafiltration (CHDF) and plasma exchange, liver function could recover. Furthermore, in pediatric cases, causes of hepatic failure are unknown in many cases. This facts lead to the difficulties to determine an indication and optimal timing of liver transplantation for children.We should be always careful to make a therapeutic decision in these setting. We have treated 10 children suffering from sever liver dysfunction from 1999. 10. to 2003.12. (3 months to 14 years old). Living donor liver transplantation (LDLT) was performed in 7 patients. Five of them are alive in 13 months to 52 months (mean: 30.6 months) after transplantation. Two patients out of 7 were lost at 10 and 4 months after operation respectively. In three patients out of 10 patients, we did not perform LDLT. Because one patient was recovered spontaneously by CHDF and plasma exchange therapy, the other two patients was diagnosed to Reye like syndrome and hemophagocytic syndrome respectively which indicated contra-indication of LDLT. We evaluated 10 patients according to the guidelines which are defined by Japan acute liver failure study group in 1996, five patients fulfilled the criteria (one patient: no need of liver transplantation, 4 patients: need for liver transplantation). In theses group, 4 patients were performed LDLT. and all the five patients were alive. On the other hand, 5 patients which did not fulfill criteria. It means that all the five patients developed severe encephalopathy in day 2 or 3. Two patients were diagnosed immediately as systemic disease and died at day 4 and day 21 respectively. Three patients were performed LDLT. Only one patient survived after LDLT. One patient developed severe cerebral edema immediately after LDLT. Conclusion: 1. The survival rate of the patients with fulminant failure patients who could keep the guideline would be very high (almost 100%). 2. Patients with fulminant hepatic failure should be considered LDLT as soon as possible when hepatic encephalopathy develops. 3. however, the systemic disease which shows hepatic failure should be neglected immediately to evaluate the contra-indication of LDLT. Department of Transplantation/Pediatric Surgery, Kumamoto University Hospital, Kumamoto, Japan [Back Ground] Immunosuppression with tacrolimus in pediatric living donor liver transplantation has been established gradually, but not completely yet. We assessed our current protocol, in which the trough level of tacrolimus was maintained less than the traditional one. [Patient & Method] Between September 2000 and November 2003, 18 children, 8 boys and 10 girls with a mean age of 83 months (ranged 2-180), received 19 living donor liver transplantations in our institution. Follow up period of 18 children ranged 4-40 months with a mean of 18. Original disease were biliary atresia in 14, and Bayler's disease, primary hyperoxaluria type I, hepatoblastoma and fulminant hepatic failure in 1 each. Immunosuppression consisted of tacrolimus and steroids. Tacrolimus protocol: 0.025 mg/kg x 2 per oral (po) on the day before transplantation. Aimed trough level of tacrolims was 10-12 ng/ml in the first week, 8-10 ng/ml in the second week, 6-8 ng/ml until one month, 5 ng/ml after one month and less than 5 ng/ml after three months. Steroid protocol: 1.0 mg/kg/day intravenously (iv) with methylpredonisolone on the first three days after liver transplantation, 0.5 mg/kg/ day iv following three days, 0.3mg/kg po with predonisolone after one week and withdrawal between three and six months after liver transplantation. [Results] Mean trough level of tacrolimus was less than 10 ng/ml in the first 3 days and then well controlled following the protocol after post-operative day 4. Two of 19 grafts were lost. One of them had a complication with bile leakage and died ten months after transplantation due to sepsis. In the other recipient, veno-occlusive disease occurred one week after the transplantation and successfully treated by re-living donor liver transplantation. Only one recipient suffered from one episode of acute rejection and there has been no episode of chronic rejection. Cytomegarovirus infection has occurred in four recipients and Epstein-Barr virus infection has occurred in two recipients. There has been no episode with posttransplant lymphoproliferated disorder. [Conclusion] Trough level of tacrolims in our current series is maintained relatively lower than the traditional one, but effectively avoided both rejection and infection, though the case number is still limited. Such a low dose protocol can be standardized in the pediatric living donor liver transplantation after confirmation by further experience. Magnetic resonance (MR) imaging plays an important role in the preoperative evaluation of the living-related liver donors to reduce postoperative morbidity and complications. Though invasive, intraoperative cholangiography is still the golden standard in demonstrating anatomical variation of bile ducts. However, there is still doubt whether intraoperative cholangiography could be replaced by non-invasive MR cholangiography. Therefore, we design this study to compare the validity and reliability between these two imaging modalities. We retrospectively searched the database of living-related liver transplantation center in National Taiwan University Hospital during the period from April 2000 to September 2003. The clinical information and images including MR cholangiography and intraoperative cholangiography of total 58 donors (female: male = 35: 23) were reviewed. We compared the visibility of branching pattern of biliary trees, detection and count of the division degree of bile ducts between these two imaging modalities. Five types of branching patterns of the bile ducts were encountered. The rate of disagreement in the classification of branching pattern of the bile ducts using MR cholangiography and intraoperative cholangiography was 5% (3/58 Pathologic changes of the recipient native portal venous system may cause thrombosis of the portal vein, especially in pediatric living donor liver transplantation (LDLT). This study assessed the utility of Doppler ultrasound for the detection of intraoperative portal vein occlusion and identification of predisposing risk factors in the recipients. 73 pediatric recipients who underwent LDLT at Chang Gung Memorial hospital, Taiwan from 1994 to 2002 were included. Pre-operative and intraoperative Doppler ultrasound (US) evaluation of the portal vein was performed. Age, body weight, native liver disease, type of graft, graft recipient weight ratio (GRWR), type of portal anastomosis, portal velocity, portal venous size and presence of portosystemic shunt were analyzed for statistical significance of predisposing risk factors. Eight episodes of intraoperative portal vein thrombosis, with typical findings of absent Doppler flow in portal vein and prominent hepatic artery with a resistant index lower than 0.5 (P<0.001), were detected during transplantation, which was then corrected by thrombectomy and re-anastomosis. Children age ≤ 1year old (P=0.025), weight ≤ 10 Kg (P=0.024), low portal flow ≤ 7cm/sec (P=0.021), portal venous size ≤ 4mm (P=0.001), and GRWR > 3 (P < 0.017) were all risk factors for intraoperative portal vein thrombosis. Doppler US is essential in the pre-operative evaluation, early detection and monitoring of outcome of the portal vein in liver transplant. SPLIT LIVER TRANSPLANT (SLT) FOR 2 ADULT RECIPIENTS -EVOLUTION OF THE SURGICAL PROCEDURE. Abhinav Humar 1 , Timothy Sielaff 1 , John R. Lake 1 , William D. Payne 1 . 1 Surgery, University of Minnesota, Minneapolis, MN, USA Background: SLT for 2 adult recipients is a procedure that is slowly developing as questions regarding donor and recipient selection are better answered. It remains a technically demanding procedure and optimizing the surgical technique of the split will play an important role in its success and ability to expand the donor pool. Results: Since 1999, we have performed 28 right lobe/left lobe splits (transplanted into 28 adult recipients) from 14 deceased donors. In the first 9 donors, the split was performed exactly similar to the way we perform adult living donor procedures. The liver was split in the midplane; the inferior vena cava was preserved with the left lobe graft, along with the middle hepatic vein and all the main hilar structures, including the common hepatic artery, portal vein, and common bile duct (CBD). In the last 5 donors, we have modified the procedure to include the cava with the right lobe graft. This has the advantage of maximizing outflow of the right lobe graft (by preserving all short hepatic veins draining into the cava) and allows back-table reconstruction of large middle hepatic vein (MHV) tributaries using vein grafts from the donor (thus minimizing warm ischemic time for the right graft at time of implantation). Outflow of the left lobe graft was then based on the common confluence of the MHV and left HV. In the last 3 donors, we have modified the split further to preserve the CBD with the right lobe graft (Fig) . Biliary drainage of both grafts is then possible with a single anastomosis in both recipients (right graft via the CBD, left graft via a single left hepatic duct). Prior to this modification, 9 of 11 right grafts required 2 separate biliary anastomosis; since then, all 3 right lobe grafts have had a single biliary anastomosis. All 14 left lobe grafts have had a single biliary anastamiosis. We have noted the incidence of biliary complications to be stongly correlated with the number of biliary anastamosis. Conclusions: The surgical procedure of adult SLT continues to undergo modifications. Optimizing the surgical technique will play a crucial part in its success. In the era of MELD, hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC) has become a prominent indication for liver transplantation. As radiofrequency ablation (RFA) has emerged as a modality in bridging patients with HCC to transplant, we have aggressively developed a minimally invasive program to optimally utilize this resource. Methods: A review of all RFA cases performed in liver transplant candidates in a single center was performed. Operative findings, morbidity and mortality data was focused upon. Results: Forty-six procedures in 45 candidates were performed. All pts were Childs class B or C. Eight pts were placed in the lateral position with 38 in the supine position. In 4 pts lesions were not well visualized utilizing a laparoscopic intraoperative ultrasound probe (IOUS). All 4 pts had a hand assist port placed and a T-bar ultrasound probe utilized. This was sufficient to visualize lesions in 3 of the patients with the remaining patient requiring a conversion to an open procedure. The mean number of lesions at exploration was 1.6. Fifteen pts had prior abdominal surgery and dense adhesions. The mean tumor size was 2.5cm. The mean estimated blood loss was 100cc. There were no peri-operative deaths. The most common complication was a transient new onset of ascites 11% (n=5) or worsening of ascites 50% (n=23). Wound complications including ascites leak 7% (n=3) and wound infection 7% (n=3) were infrequent. 67% (n=31) patients were listed for transplantation and 52% (n=24) have been transplanted thus far. Two pts who underwent transplantation had subsequent cardiac complications and died after OLT. Explanted liver tumors have been documented to have 30%-99% necrosis. Six deaths (43%) were observed in the group of pts not listed for transplant. Causes of death included: hepatic failure (n=2), tumor progression (n=3), and massive variceal bleed (n=1). Conclusion: The laparoscopic approach for RFA offers a safe and effective approach for the evaluation and treatment of HCC. With the addition of the hand assist technique the great majority of patients are able to undergo RFA without conversion to a formal open procedure. An effective bridge to transplantation, patients receive adequate MELD scores for subsequent transplantation without incurring excessive morbidity or mortality. Introduction: Identification of malignant lesions in cirrhotic patients increase priority scores for these patients based on the MELD system. Often diagnosis and treatment must be based on frozen section results from a cirrhotic liver. Radiofrequency ablation (RFA) is a promising treatment for such tumors in the pre transplant period providing interval therapy prior to transplantation Methods: Review of all patients at a single transplant institution who underwent RFA for tumor treatment and subsequently underwent liver transplantation. All patients had frozen and permanent section pathological biopsies evaluated for the presence of tumor. These results were compared to the final pathology after hepatectomy for liver transplantation. Results: 16 patients were identified who underwent abdominal exploration and RFA with open biopsy of known liver tumors prior to liver transplantation. All patients had cirrhotic liver disease. The cause of liver disease and cirrhosis was hepatitis C (69%), hepatitis B (12%), hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC), alcohol and crytogenic cirrhosis (6% each). Preoperative MELD score was a mean of 12.4 ± 7.6. Of the 16 patients, 14 were identified at time of operation as having HCC on frozen section pathology (88%), although one patient later proved to have cholangiocarcinoma variant of HCC. Two patients identified as having no tumor at biopsy were subsequently shown to have HCC at time of hepatectomy (12%). One patient had a hemangioma incorrectly identified as atypia and one patient had HCC incorrectly identified as normal tissue. Each patient had an average of 2 tumors (range 1 to 6) located in either the right lobe (11 patients) or both lobes (5 patients). Tumor size averaged 3.1 centimeters in diameter. After exploration, RFA and biopsy, the MELD score mean increased to 23.4 ± 6.4 (two-fold increase). At a median follow up time of 7 months all patients remain alive. Conclusions: Abdominal exploration frozen section biopsy and RFA is an aggressive approach to cirrhotic patients with undiagnosed liver masses. At our institution this procedure achieved a tumor identification sensitivity of 88% and specificity of 100%. The practice of tumor staging by RFA and biopsy resulted in more accurate MELD scoring and led to definitive treatment by liver transplantation. (HCC) is now the fifth most common cancer world wide, is the third cause of cancer-related death and is increasing from past years in the United States. The greatest risk factors for developing HCC remains to be hepatitis. Prognostic estimation is a question of tumor staging, residual liver function and co-morbidities of the patient. The natural history of HCC has been historically based upon data collected prior to adequate diagnostic tools and serum markers of cancer. Because of this, survival estimations and recommendations of therapy underestimate a patient's survivability with current therapies. It is not clear if preoperative imaging studies accurately define the state of tumor burden. We hypothesize that there is no difference in accuracy between extracorporeal preoperative imaging and intra-corporeal operative US (IOUS) of liver tumors. 68 patients were evaluated for liver tumor(s) and received open or laparoscopic radiofrequency ablation (RFA) between 12/2000 and 9/2003. All patients were diagnosed with a liver tumor(s) by either ultrasound (US), computed tomography (CT) or magnetic resonance imaging (MRI). All patients had pathologic verification of malignancy at the time of RFA. Twenty one patients proceeded on to liver transplantation (Ltx). Pathology of the tumor at the time of RFA and on explant were compared. 68 patients with 92 liver masses had RFA performed. Preoperative imaging found the average tumor size to be 1.9 cm by US, 4.0 cm by CT and 2.9 cm by MRI. IOUS assessment found a maximal tumor diameter to be 3.47cm (p<0.05, P=0.09, P=0.42, respectively). Twenty-one (30.9%) patients proceeded on to Ltx. This cohort had an average of 1.23 tumors per liver and mean diameter 2.9cm. Intra-corporeal US significantly differed from all pre-operative imaging results (US, 1.8cm; CT, 4.5cm; and MRI, 4.9cm; P<0.05) . Accurate identification and tumor sizing is critical in surgical decision making for cirrhotic patients with tumors. Preoperative staging has been inaccurate because of the cirrhotic liver parenchyma characteristics; distinguishing viable tumor from regenerating nodules has been challenging. This study identifies that pre-operative US, CT and MRI poorly assesses the true tumor burden. This would advocate for IOUS interrogation of liver tumors in cirrhotic patients prior to medical decision making to better assign therapies to maximize outcomes for these patients. LT with preservation of the inferior vena cava (IVC), known as piggy-back (PB) technique offers several advantages: no need for a veno-venous bypass (VVB), shorter operation time and less peroperative blood loss. However no consensus exists regarding the optimal reconstruction of the venous outflow tract (VOTR). Several modifications of VOTR have been described. We report our experiences with end-to-side (ES) caval anastomosis in adult LT, particularly in terms of complications specific to this PB technique. A consecutive series (1995) (1996) (1997) (1998) (1999) (2000) (2001) (2002) of 149 adult LT in 141 patients using ES caval anastomosis were analyzed, which represents 50% of all (n=299) adult LT performed in this period. In all cases an end-to-side VOTR was done as follows: (1) during recipient hepatectomy orifices of the right, middle and left hepatic veins were closed with running suture; (2) the caudal end of the donor IVC was closed and its cranial end was left open and prolonged by longitudinal midline incision in the posterior wall; (3) after clamping of the recipient IVC, the anterior wall was incised longitudinally and the graft was placed orthotopically, and rolled to the right; (4) the anastomosis between donor and recipient IVC was performed using two running sutures. VVB was not used in any case and a temporary portocaval shunt was performed in 9 patients (6%). Twenty-seven patients (19%) died during follow up, but death was not related to the procedure. All surviving patients had a minimal follow up of one year. Median follow-up was 2,6 years (range 0.0-8.6 years). One, three and five year patient and graft survival were 84%, 80%, 80% and 80%, 72%, and 69%, respectively. AHP, warm ischemia time, revascularization time and total operation time were 78 min (range 43-207), 50 min (range 20-130), 89 min (range 43-217), and 552 min (range 255-890), respectively. Intraoperative use of packed red blood cells was 2,0 (range 0-33) and 30% patients did not require transfusion. Seventy patients (49%) had a postoperative complication. In only one case the complication was specifically related to the technique. Our experiences indicate that piggyback LT with ES caval anastomosis is a safe procedure with a low incidence of technique-related complications. As we have a very low incidence of major biliary complications( 2,94 %) with a reoperation rate of 1, 23 % we consider safe and useful using T-Tube drainage In a retrospective study, we identified the patients (pts) with those hepatic artery thrombosis (HAT) -that we called "very late HAT" -arising more than 3 years after liver transplantation (LT): our aim was to check what kind of diagnostic tool was useful in the diagnosis of very late HAT. Patients and methods In our Unit, since 1983 through January 2004, 603 consecutive LT, with 72 Retransplantations (RETX), were performed in 531 adult and pediatric pts. The protocol of surveillance for HAT included Doppler Ultrasonography (US), and, in case of diagnostic doubts, computed tomography (CT) scan with arterial reconstructions, and/or hepatic arteriography. Doppler US was performed daily, soon after LT, and then monthly and yearly, unless the onset of rising values of liver enzymes. Patients with very late HAT were reviewed, focusing attention to diagnostic tools to detect intrahepatic arterial flow, and outcome. Results 24 HAT were recorded, 19 of them arising within 3 years after LT. So, 5 adult pts underwent 5 very late HAT, that is 0,8% of LT, 20% of HAT. Mean time of onset of very late HAT was 10, 3 ± 4,4 years (range 3,7 -14,9) . All patients with very late HAT had mild fever with biochemical signs of cholestasis. Doppler US always assessed the presence of intrahepatic arterial flow. On the contrary, TC scan reported, together with a dilatation of the intrahepatic biliary tree sometimes with abscesses, the right diagnosis, with complete HAT and no arterial intrahepatic flow. Angiography confirmed TC scan results. 3 patients underwent a successful RETX (4,1% of RETX), whereas 2 died of sepsis before surgery. Discussion and Conclusion Very late HAT after LT has a low rate, probably destined to increase due to the increasing number of long time transplanted pts. It must be suspected when a rise of values of hepatic enzymes with mild clinical signs of sepsis occur, without a clear explanation. Doppler US has a misleading pattern, as arterial intrahepatic inefficient flow detected by Doppler US is probably due to diaphragmatic vessels, so it can be argued that the rate of very late HAT is probably underestimated. CT Scan with arterial reconstructions can correctly detect the diagnosis. The usefulness of low-cost contrast enhanced US needs to be verified. Conclusion: While multiple and often times, severe complications can occur following LTx, the overall survival of these patients is excellent.
