TECHNICAL REFINEMENTS IN EX-SITU SPLIT LIVER. Silvio Balzan 1 , Olivier Farges 1 , Federica Dondero 1 , Daniele Sommacale 1 , Satoshi Ogata 1 , Marylène Plasse 1 , Jacques Belghiti 1 . 1 Hepatobiliary Surgery and Transplantation, Beaujon Hospital, Clichy, France Background: The split-liver is an important technique to alleviate donor shortage. However, the number of split grafts has not increased over the past 5 years. Besides difficulty in organization, other limitations for split-liver transplantation include the additional time required to prepare the graft, longer ischemic time and technical difficulty, with increased incidence of biliary complications in some series, in particular with ex-situ splitting. Biliary complications can be related to ischemia (due to extensive hilar dissection) or section of multiple biliary ducts (due to biliary blind section). The aim of this study is to describe a technical refinement to alleviate those drawbacks by a clear identification of hilar biliary anatomy with minimal hilar dissection using methylene blue in ex-situ split liver. Method: The whole organ is retrieved and preserved with Wisconsin solution according to standard techniques and a cholangiography is performed to identify major anatomical biliary variations. The parenchyma transection is then performed (along the main portal scissura or umbilical fissure) prior to any hilar or pedicular dissection. The hilar plate is thus exposed but neither dissected nor encircled. Methylene blue 1% diluted in physiological serum is then injected in the biliary tract by a metallic cannula inserted in the distal extremity of the common bile duct. This maneuver allows a very clear visualization of all large and small biliary structures at the hilar level. The best site for the biliary duct partition is selected and the section is performed without previous vascular dissection. Results: This technique was used in 5 split-liver transplantations. Optimal visualization of hilar large and small bile ducts was achieved in all procedures and the best site for biliary section was identified with minimal dissection. A single biliary anastomosis was possible in all 10 recipients. Biliary complications were identified in only 1 (10%) patient who had a biliary fistula in postoperative period. The time required to liver partition was shorter than with standard technique (45 vs 60 minutes respectively). Conclusion: The parenchymal transection performed prior to any hilar dissection and the injection of methylene blue in ex-situ split liver is a simple technique that allows clear visualization of hilar bile ducts. Optimal site of biliary section can be identified without extensive dissection. During orthotopic liver transplantation (OLT) with inferior vena cava (IVC) preservation several technical modifications related to the outflow cavo-caval anastomosis have been proposed. As suggested by Parrilla and Belghiti an end to side outflow anastomosis created with the patch of the three native suprahepatic veins seem to be associated with a lower incidence of graft related venous outflow complications. Nevertheless, caval clamping that includes the three suprahepatic veins is sometimes technically difficult and requires transient IVC cross-clamping expecially in patients with a large liver. We describe a modification of Belghiti's liver hanging maneuver applied to the last phase of hepatectomy during OLT with IVC preservation. The lifting of the liver provides a better exposure of the suprahepatic region and allows easier orthogonal clamping of the three suprahepatic veins that occlude only a minimal portion of IVC. In the proposed modification the blind dissection of the anterior part of the IVC is limited to the region between the right and middle hepatic veins, minimizing the risk of this maneuver and exposing the suprahepatic veins for clamping. In 30 consecutive OLT with IVC preservation the modified liver hanging maneuver was achieved in all cases. Caval clamping including the three hepatic veins with preservation of the caval flow was achieved in all cases. The graft outflow anastomosis was performed between the graft IVC and the constructed common cloaca of the 3 hepatic veins in 28 cases and with the right hepatic stump enlarged to a minimal part of IVC in 2 cases Post-operative graft function and ascites were recorded on postoperative day 1, 3 5, 7 and 10 as showed in the table. The proposed modified hanging maneuver provides a better exposure of the three suprahepatic veins during OLT with IVC preservation. This provides a large outflow anastomotic cloaca that includes the right hepatic vein and preserves caval flow. Graft function and ascites on post-operative day 1, 3 5, 7 <Introduction> Lethal liver dysfunction induced by Extended hepatectomy could be caused by various and complicated factors, one of which is considered that the entire whole portal blood directed toward the small remnant liver. Furthermore, in adult liver transplantation, the similar impairment may be occured in cases with small for size graft. Accordingly, we hypothesized that resection of the small intestine would reduce portal pressure, and would become a strategy to overcome the problem. <Materials and Methods> Fourteen beagle dogs of either gender aging around one year were elected for this study. Dogs were randomly divided into Groups A and B. Group A was consisted of 70% hepatectomised dogs with transection and reanastomosis of the small intestine. Group B was consisted of dogs with 70% hepatectomy after 50% proximal resection of the small intestine. Because, death of each one dog from Group A and B severally, 6 dogs of each group were examined, and 3 dogs were euthanized in 1 and 4 weeks, respectively of each group. Effect of 50% resection of the small intestine on portal pressure after the 70% hepatectomy was investigated of the hemodynamic changes, liver functions, and histology of the liver. <Results> Significant decrease of the portal pressure in Group B with 50% enterectomy was revealed compared with that in Group A (P=0.002). On the first postoperative day, AST, ALT, ALP, HPT, ALB and AT3 in Group B were significantly more profitable than those in Group A. Total hepatic blood flow was significantly higher in Group B than Group A (P=0.037) after the hepatectomy. Histopathological findings of the remnant liver biopsied at one hour after the hepatectomy in Group A showed more severe swollen hepatocytes than those in Group B. Although, the remnant liver weights at 1 or 4 weeks in Group B was lighter than that in Group A. More PCNA positive hepatocytes in Group B at 1 and 4 weeks seemed to mean gradual and prolonged proliferations. Furthermore, the other findings of tissue flow and TUNEL stain results in Group B were superior than those in Group A. <Conclusion> Our results suggest that control of portal pressure by the enterectomy can a countermeasure to avoid not only residual liver dysfunction after extended hepatectomy but small for size graft syndrome after living liver transplantation in adults. Background: Early and late biliary tract complications after cadaveric orthotopic liver transplantation still remain serious morbidity and mortality. The choledocho-jejunostomy and the duct-to-duct biliary reconstruction with stent or t-tube have been commonly performed as the standard methods. Recently, the duct-to-duct biliary reconstruction without stent nor t-tube has been used. The aim of this study is to evaluate the early and late complication rate and outcome of duct-to-duct biliary reconstruction without stent nor t-tube after cadaveric liver transplantation for adult patients. Methods: Between February 1996 and December 2003, 266 adult patients underwent 268 cadeveric orthotopic whole liver transplantations with duct-to-duct biliary reconstruction without stent nor t-tube. Most of the anastomoses were performed in continuous fashion using reabsobable sutures. Clinical data were obtained by retrospective review of medical records and computerized database. Results: Three biliary leakage (1.1 %) was observed within the early post operative period (POD 2 -5) after liver transplantation. Fifteen stenosis (5.6%)of the common bile duct anastomosis were observed within the early post operative period (POD3-8). Seven stenosis (2.6 %) of the common bile duct anastomosis were observed at late post operative period (POD54-1109). Of the 7 patients, 4 patients had hepatic artery thrombosis. Three non-anastomotic obstruction (1.1%) of the common bile duct were observed at early post operative period (POD7-11). Complications unrelated to the surgical technique, including two intra-hepatic biliary stricture (POD5, 82), one papillary stenosis (POD197) and one biliary stone (POD23) were observed. There was no mortality in this study group due to the biliary complication during this study period. There were 5 morbidities (3 early stenosis, 1 late stenosis and 1 biliary stone) in the last 100 patients. Conclusions: Duct-to-duct biliary reconstruction without stent nor t-tube represents a useful technique in cadaveric liver transplantation for adult patients. However, biliary stenosis remains as a technical related complication. This study analyses the impact of this change on peri-operative parameters, renal support, complications and outcome. Methods: All data were prospectively collected on a dedicated data-base. A consecutive series of 384 primary liver transplants (2000) (2001) (2002) (2003) were analysed. Results: (table) The requirement of respiratory support was higher (P=0.03) in the CON-LT group. There was no difference in the requirement of renal support. 30 day patient survival was 89% for CON-LT and 93% for CCP-LT (P=1.4;NS). Temporary porto-caval shunts (PCS) were used in 192 (78%) patients who underwent CCP-LT. The outcome parameters between those who had PCS and those who did not were not significant. However the group without a temporary PCS had a significantly lower warm ischemia time (P=0.03). Introduction: After small-for-size liver transplantation with a remnant liver volume of less than 0,5% of body weight often a severe portal hyperperfusion has been noticed. The influence of this portal hyperperfusion to the postoperative liver dysfunction has been discussed controversially. Several experimental and clinical studies tried to reduced the portal overflow by surgical shunt or ligation of the splenic artery. We report about our first experience with the Transjugular Intrahepatic Portosystemic Shunt (TIPS) for temporary portal decompression in a small-for-size Mini-pig model. Material and methods: 20 Mini-pigs between 24 and 40 kg underwent laparotomy under general anaesthesia. The study was authorized by the German government and is conform with 1991 Revision of Guiding Principles in the Care and Use of Animals. In all animals extended resection of 75 % of liver volume (left lateral lobe, left medial lobe, right medial lobe) using crash-clamp technique was performed followed by the introduction of the TIPS (6 mm Easy-Wall Stent, Titan, 43 mm length) in the remnant liver (25% of total liver volume). TIPS was placed between right hepatic vein and main portal vein by fluoroscopic control and finger guidance. Anticoagulation was performed with 10.000 iE Heparin intraveniously during placement followed by low molecular Heparin daily. Portal vein and TIPS flow velocity was measured 1 and 72 hours after resection intraoperatively by 12 MHz Doppler-Duplex ultrasound. Results: The additional time for placing the TIPS was 60 minutes in average. In 17 animals (85%) the TIPS was successfully placed. In one case no hepatic vein was found for catheterisation (5%), two animals died due to blood loss immediately after resection (10% There was no stenosis of the anastomosis between the right hepatic vein and the IVC; however, intra-hepatic stenosis of the hepatic venous trunk like twisting form and a pressure gradient of 14 mmHg at the stenosis was revealed by hepatic venogram and manometry. We placed an expandable metallic stent (EMS) at the intra-hepatic venous stenosis, and succeeded in decreasing pressure. Liver function recovered and the amount of ascites decreased. The patient was discharged on the 41rd postoperative day, and is currently well 9 months after the operation. Case 2: A 51-year-old male developed outflow block18 days after LDLT for liver cirrhosis with hepatitis C. There was a stenosis of the anastomosis between the hepatic vein and the IVC, and the gradient was 10 mm Hg. We placed an EMS at the stenosis. The patient had been well after then to discharge on the 38rd postoperative day, and is currently well 8 months after the operation. Cases 3 and 4: Case 3 is a 54-year old male who had a LDLT for liver cirrhosis with hepatitis C; Case 4 is a 51-year-old male also with liver cirrhosis with hepatitis C. Both had LDLT performed with anastomosis of the right hepatic, inferior right hepatic, and major hepatic veins from segments V or VIII. They presented with outflow block. Veno cavographies demonstrated stenosis of the IVC and gradients were 13 mm Hg and 11 mm Hg; so we placed IVC Gianturco stents at 10 and 20 postoperative days, respectively, in the two patients. Liver function recovered and the amount of ascites decreased. Case 3, unfortunately, died due to severe gastrointestinal bleeding from a duodenal ulcer on the 77th postoperative day. Case 4 was discharged on the 43rd postoperative day, and is currently well 6 months after the operation. Early portal vein thrombosis is a rare but severe complication of liver transplantation requiring retransplantation or sometimes leading to death. We describe a successful reflow after early surgical intervention and pharmacological lysis of intrahepatic thrombus by direct injection of urokinase solution into the portal vein. A 56-year-old Japanese female affected by primary biliary cirrhosis underwent living related liver transplantation using right lobe graft from her son. The recipient had preexisting portal vein thrombosis from the portal trunk to the confluence of the splenic vein and superior mesenteric vein before transplantation. The portal vein was narrowed by chronic inflammatory tissue. The thrombus was removed, and the stricture was released by removing the scar tissue, and portal vein reconstruction was carried out. Good portal flow was demonstrated by ultrasound colordoppler examination immediate after operation, however, the portal flow was not detected on day 1 post transplantation. Laparotomy was performed emergently and fist-size hematoma was found around portal vein compressing the portal trunk below the anastomosis. Removing hematoma, the portal vein was opened to reveal thrombus locating from confluence of splenic vein/SMV to intrahepatic branches of right portal vein. The portal vein was recanalized by removal of thrombus in the portal truck and injection of 24,000 U of Urokinase directly into the intrahepatic portal vein. Within 30 min of this procedure intrahepatic portal vein was visualized and good portal blood flow was secured on US. Continuous urokinase infusion (12,000 U/day) through a middle colic vein was started during the operation and continued for 3 days. Activated clotting time was maintained between 200 to 250 second by infusion of heparin from the same catheter until day 16, when warfarin was started in replace of heparin. After 2 months of follow-up, the patient is in good clinical condition with normal graft function and good portal flow.This case report confirms the possibility of successful recanalization of the portal vein with thrombus by surgical removal of the stricture and urokinase injection through the portal vein. Placement of infusion catheter into a branch of SMV and direct infusion of anticoagulant drugs into portal vein could help to decrease the risk of recurrent thrombosis. The risk of developing "de novo" malignancies after liver transplantation is around 1% per year. The incidence varies from 3 to 15%, and it is greater than that in the general population. The potential causes for cancer after solid organ grafting are: chronic immunosuppression and viral. Objective: The goals of this paper were to review the medical literature about the subject and to verify the incidence of "de novo" malignancies in our service. Methods: A retrospective analysis of the medical files of 264 successive patients submitted to orthotopic liver transplantation from September 1991 to January 2004 was performed. Type of tumor, the risk factors involved, the treatment modality and the patient survival were registered and analyzed. The recurrence of hepatocellular carcinoma was excluded. There were 5 (1.8%) cases, four men and one woman, the average age being 39.5. The type of tumor, the age of each patient, time from liver transplantation to malignancy diagnosis and the survival of each patient are noted. Results: There was a 60% mortality. The survival was affected by the benign nature of one of the tumors and by the early manifestation of intestinal obstruction in another allowing adequate surgical treatment. Three of the patients were heavy alcohol consumers before the transplant. Conclusion: The screening for premalignant lesions must be strongly encouraged, aiming at better post-operative results. Background Donor right hepatectomy subjects a healthy donor to an ultra-major surgical operation only for the benefit of the recipient. This has become a standard treatment modality to partially relieve the refractory shortage of cadaveric liver grafts. Justification of this procedure ought to be made with the full knowledge of the potential negative effects on the donor. Psychosocial assessment and monitoring of the donor has to be an integral part of such program. Aim of study To quantify the impact of donor right hepatectomy including the middle hepatic vein on donor quality of life before and after surgery longitudinally. Materials and methods Questionnaires with condition specific and generic components were completed by a face-to-face interview by one of the two full-time liver transplantation coordinators before operation, and 1, 2, 3, 6, and 12 months after operation. The generic instruments used were Karnofsky Performance Status, and the Chinese (Hong Kong) version of the Medical Outcomes Study 36-Item Short-form Survey. The latter measures eight concepts: physical functioning, physical role, emotional role, social functioning, bodily pain, mental health, vitality, and general health. Condition specific questions were also included. Results Between November 2002 and November 2003, 30 consecutive donors who underwent donor right hepatectomy including the middle hepatic vein were assessed and monitored prospectively before and after surgery. They were aged 35 years (21 to 56), with male to female ratio of 33.3%:66.7%. The median follow-up was 6 months (2 to 12). All except 1 were Chinese (1 Indian). There was no donor mortality or major complications. All the donors completed the questionnaires on schedule. Donor quality of life before surgery was excellent physically as they were by definition healthy individuals. The worsening was most significant in the 1 st to 3 rd month post-operatively, especially in the domains of physical functioning and bodily pain. These and other psychological aspects returned to the previous levels in 6 months' time. 86.7% (N=26) of these donors reckoned that their donor experience was positive. Conclusion Donor quality of life was adversely affected immediately following surgery. Return to previous levels could be expected at 6 month in large majority of donors. Live donor liver transplantation seems to be acceptable for the rescue of recipients with end-stage liver disease as adverse changes in donor quality of health is temporary. The MELD/PELD (M/P) system for liver allocation was implemented on February 27 2002. Reporting of 6-month post transplant survival data is 84% complete for the 4204 liver transplants performed with deceased donors from 2/27/02 through 12/31/02. For recipients transplanted at M/P scores, actual 6-month patient survival was 91.38% (CI=90.40,92.25) that was significantly better than the 79.5% (CI=75.46,83.55) survival rate for status 1 candidates. Overall 6-month graft survival for M/P recipients (87.56, CI=86.49, 88.62) was also superior to the status 1 recipients (74.66%, CI=70.38,78.94). Pediatric graft survival (88.16%, CI=84.17,92.14) was similar to adults (87.51, CI=86.40,88.62). When stratified by PELD score at transplant, pediatric recipients had excellent 6-month patient survival with no difference in patient survival rates among the strata. Pediatric graft survival was also not different among the PELD strata. However when adults are stratified by MELD score at transplant, recipients with MELD>35 had acceptable but inferior patient and graft survival compared with the other strata. Adult recipients with HCC MELD exceptions had improved 6-month patient survival (90.70%, CI=88.73,92.67) over non-HCC exceptions (86.46%, CI=82.40,90.52) and standard MELD (86.64%, CI=82.40,90.52) recipients but these differences were not statistically significant. We conclude that early post transplant patient and graft survival remains excellent under the MELD/PELD system with even the most ill adult and pediatric chronic disease recipients achieving 84.3% and 86.8% 6-month patient survival rates respectively. 4 Gastroenterology & Hepatology, University Hospital Groningen, Groningen, Netherlands Biliary complications are a cause of morbidity and graft dysfunction after liver transplantation. Ischemia and reperfusion play an important role in the occurrence of bile duct injury. Animal studies have shown that administration of bile salts can seriously amplify preservation injury of the biliary tree. Bile salts have detergent properties and their cytotoxic effects are antagonized by phospholipids. The role of bile salt secretion and changes in bile composition on the occurrence of bile duct injury, however, has not been studied before. We investigated changes in the secretion of bile salts and phospholipids after human liver transplantation and correlated these with biochemical markers of bile duct injury. In addition, expression of bile salt uptake (NTCP, Na + -dependent taurocholate cotransporting polypeptide) and secretion (BSEP, bile salt export pump) systems, as well as the phospholpid translocator (MDR3, multidrug resistance protein 3), was studied. Three sequential biopsies were taken from liver grafts in 28 patients: at the end of cold storage, 3 hours after reperfusion, and 1 week after transplantation. Blood and bile samples were collected daily until 13 days after transplantation. Serum concentrations of ALP and γ -GT, as well as the release of these enzymes into the bile, were used as markers of bile duct cell injury. After transplantation, bile salt secretion restored rapidly, whereas phospholipid secretion increased at a slower rate, resulting in a significantly higher bile salt/phospholipid ratio in bile formed on day 1 compared to day 13. In parallel with this, NTCP and BSEP mRNA levels increased significantly after transplantation, whereas MDR3 mRNA levels remained unchanged, in comparison with gene expression before transplantation or in normal control livers. Bile duct cell injury, as reflected by ALP and γ -GT release into the bile, correlated significantly with bile salt secretion. Conclusion: Bile salt secretion is rapidly restored after transplantation and is disproportionately higher than the initial secretion of phospholipids. This results in the formation cytotoxic bile. These findings suggest that endogenous bile salts have a role in the pathogenesis of intrahepatic bile duct damage after liver transplantation. Extended criteria donor (ECD) liver utilization has expanded the limited donor pool for patients awaiting orthotopic liver transplant (OLT). Many centers approach ECD livers with caution as the outcomes following their use remains unclear. PURPOSE: To analyze outcomes of OLT using ECD livers compared to a control group of standard criteria donor (SCD) liver allografts. METHODS: All OLT recipients at our center between Jan 2002-Oct 2003 were categorized as having received an ECD or SCD liver. ECD allografts were defined as having any one or more of the following: donor age > 60 yrs, biopsy exhibiting >40% macrovesicular and/or >75% microvesicular steatosis, any donor down time (±CPR), use of 2 or more pressors within 12 hrs of procurement, or liver function studies > 5 x upper limit of normal. Parameters that were reviewed included: recipient demographics (age, sex, MELD, mean follow-up, length of stay), graft ischemic times (total and warm), laboratory data, and clinical outcomes (death, biliary complications, rejections). RESULTS: 90 OLTs (42 ECD and 48 SCD) were performed during the study period. No statistical differences were observed in laboratory data including: serum creatinine, transaminases (AST/ALT), total bilirubin, and albumin at discharge, day 30, 90, and 180 for both groups. The two groups were similar with respect to rejection episodes per patient ECD (1.1) vs. SCD (1.3) [ p=ns]. Five instances of vascular complications were identified (3 ECD vs. 2 SCD), although only one of those was a hepatic artery thrombosis. CONCLUSIONS: Clinical outcomes following OLT using ECD livers are identical to those using SCD livers. No differences were noted insofar as early or mid laboratory values, LOS, biliary or vascular complications. With intelligent recipient selection, the aggressive use of ECD liver allografts can become an important component of any transplant program. In order to expand the donor pool, there has been an increasing number of steatotic livers used for transplantation. It is known that the use of livers with significant macrosteatosis poses a higher risk for primary non-function and early graft failure. However, the long term effect on allograft and patient survival after transplantation of livers with macrosteatosis is not known. In this study, we wanted to determine: 1) if there was a higher incidence of late graft failure, and 2) whether there was a higher incidence and more severe recurrence of hepatitis C in liver transplant patients who received fatty livers. A retrospective analysis of 736 patients who underwent 829 liver transplants (93 retransplants) between January 1, 1994 and December 31, 1997 was performed. Of these cases, 142 donor biopsies in 133 patients were performed, and these comprise the study group. The mean follow-up for the patients was 5.9 ± 4.0 years with an overall patient and graft survival of 57% and 54%, respectively. Sixty-two of the biopsies (58 patients) showed histologic findings of macrosteatosis (Group I) and 81 biopsies (78 patients) had no evidence of macrosteatosis (Group II). Both groups were comparable with respect to recipient age, gender, UNOS status, and etiology of liver disease as well as donor age and cold ischemia time. The overall patient survival for Group I and Group II was 48% and 63% [p=NS], respectively, and graft survival was 48% (Group I) and 56% (Group II) [p=NS] . There was no difference between Group I and Group II in either the early (<1 year) (34% vs 30%, respectively, p=NS) or late (> 1 year) (18% vs 16%, respectively, p=NS) graft failure rates. Fifteen patients in Group I and 28 patients in Group II had hepatitis C and there was no difference in patient and graft survival between the 2 groups. Bridging fibrosis or cirrhosis has developed in 3 of 15 (20%) patients in Group I and 8 of 28 (29%) patients in Group II (p=NS). Patients in Group I were divided into those who received grafts with >30% (6 patients), 20-30% (16 patients), and <20% (36 patients) macrosteatosis. We found no difference in the patient and graft survival in these patients. CONCLUSION: Even with selected macrosteatotic donors, long term patient and graft survival after liver transplantation is satisfactory. In addition, the use of fatty livers did not appear to affect the incidence or severity of recurrent hepatitis C. Immunosuppression may increase the risk of non-skin malignancy after liver transplantation (LT) and malignancy may increase mortality. Aim. To study the incidence of non-skin malignancy after LT, to identify its risk factors and to evaluate its impact on patient survival. Patients and methods. The incidence of non-skin malignancy after LT was studied in 187 consecutive patients surviving more than 6 months after their first LT (May'90-October'01). The patients were followed until 2003. Risk factors were studied by Cox analysis. The impact of malignancy on survival was studied in a case-control study (2 controls per case). Results. Twenty-seven patients developed non-skin malignancy. The actuarial 1-, 3-, 5-, 7-and 10-year incidences of non-skin malignancy were 1%, 5%, 14%, 20% and 24%. In multivariate analysis, risk factors associated to the development of non-skin malignancy were a higher age, history of cigarette smoking and history of high alcohol intake before transplantation. Actuarial 1-, 3-, 5-, 7-and 10-year survival rates of patients with non-skin malignancy were significantly lower than survival rates of patients without neoplasia (93%, 74%, 66%, 49% and 42% vs 100%, 98%, 96%, 93% and 82%, respectively; P < 0.0001). Fifteen of the 27 patients with non-skin neoplasia died (median after diagnosis of malignancy: 5 months) and 12 of them are alive (median: 27.5 months). Conclusions: Non-skin malignancy is a frequent complication after LT. It is related to age, alcohol and cigarette smoking. Non-skin malignancy reduces post-LT survival. Roberto Troisi 1 , Roberto Montalti 1 , Salvatore Ricciardi 1 , Hans Van Vlierberghe 2 , Isabelle Colle 2 , Bernard de Hemptinne 1 . 1 General, Hepato-Biliary and Transplantation Surgery; 2 Dept. of Hepato-Gastroenterology, Ghent University Hospital Medical School, Ghent, Belgium Background. Biliary complications in adult-to-adult living donor liver transplantation (AALDLT) are the major causes leading to early and long-term post-transplant morbidity. We analyze retrospectively the incidence, risk factors and overall morbidity of a single centre experience. Material and methods. From September 1999 to October 2003, 53 consecutive AALDLT were performed in our institution (47 Right Livers and 6 Left Livers). The M/F ratio was 32/21 and the mean age was of 54y (range 19-66). Indications were: n=22 (41%) viral cirrhosis; n=19 (36%) HCC on cirrhosis; n=2 (4%) PBC; n=2 (4%) PSC; n=8 (15%) other. Mean GRBWR was of 1,13 (range 0.56-1.93). Preoperative biliary anatomy in donors was done either by Cholangio-MRCP (53%) or Cholangio-CT (47%). Results. The median FU was of 13 m. Variations in biliary anatomy were encountered in 55% of RL donors whereas none occurred in LL. Biliary reconstructions were done as follows: Hepatico-Choledoco (HC) in 36 pts (68%), Roux-en-Y (RY) in 16 pts (30%), combined in 1 (2%). Single ducts anastomoses were done in 37 (75%) pts, multiple (2) (3) (4) in 13 (25%). Bilioplasty permitting a single duct anastomosis was needed in 7 (13%) pts. There were 20 complications in 18 (34%) pts: 35% in HC and 31% in RY respectively (p=ns). Early BC were observed in 15 (28%) pts: n=6(30%) non-anastomotic leak (3 revisions, 2 conservative, 1 PTC); n=5(25%) anastomotic fistulas (4 surgical revisions and 1 conservative); n=4(20%) peritonitis from T-tube dislocation (2 surgical revisions). Late BC (stenosis) were observed in 5 (25%) pts and required 3 dilatations and 2 stents. Late stenosis (27 m) in one patient led to sepsis and graft loss. Following factors were not significant prognostic factors: number of anastomoses, type of liver, biliary drainage, MELD score, GRBWR. The incidence of BC decreased after the 20th AALDLT (40% vs. 30%) (p=0.8). Overall 4-years patient and graft survival is 80% and 72% respectively. No statistical difference was found between recipients who experienced biliary complications and those who did not. Conclusions. BC represent the major cause of early and long-term morbidity in AALDLT. HC anastomosis is possible also in case of multiple ducts. BC seems to be independent from the learning curve; however, one graft was lost due to late BC. A longer FU is needed to address exactly the incidence of graft loss for late biliary complications in AALDLT. Johan Ungerstedt 1 , 2 , Grzegorz Nowak 2 , Bo-Goran Ericzon 2 , Urban Ungerstedt 1 . 1 Physiology and Pharmacology, Karolinska Institutet, Stockholm, Sweden; 2 Transplantation Surgery, Karolinska Institutet, Huddinge, Sweden The liver has a central role in the metabolism of amino acids. Amino acids may therefore serve as indicators of hepatic graft functional status after orthotopic liver transplantation. The microdialysis technique gives the opportunity of intra hepatic monitoring of amino acids from the extra cellular fluid by introducing a small catheter into the liver. In the present study we monitored intra hepatic amino acids postoperatively in 10 patients with microdialysis in order to describe levels and changes of amino acids in the liver early after transplantation in humans. Ten consecutive patients undergoing whole organ orthotopic liver transplantation were studied, 4 due to hepatitis C cirrhosis, 3 with familial amyloidotic polyneuropathy (FAP), 2 with primary sclerosing cholangitis and 1 with primary biliary cirrhosis. The microdialysis catheter was introduced into segment IV of the transplanted liver at the end of the operation. Monitoring of the liver continued for three days and amino acids where analysed by HPLC with fluorescence detection. The levels of Aspartate, Citrulline, Glutamate, and Taurine were initially high (9.7, 71, 169, and 116 mM respectively) and decreased during the first three postoperative days to 2.3, 40, 22, and 39 mM respectively. Arginine, Aspargine, Lysine, Ornithine, Serine, and Threonine increased during the period from 31, 47, 170, 78, 69, and 91 mM to 30, 98, 646, 220, 103, and 207 mM. However, Alanine, Glutamine, Glycine, Isoleucine, Leucine, Phenylalanine, Tyrosine, and Valine together with Methionine were stable during the monitoring period. One patient experienced rejection and cholangitis one week after transplantation and finally required retransplantation 4 months later. This patient had a diverging amino acid pattern in the Arginine/Citrulline (Arg/Cit) ratio, already during the first four hours postoperatively. This is the first time intra hepatic amino acids have been monitored in humans. We conclude that it is possible to measure amino acids and that the amino acids changed differently during the postoperative recovery face. We did not experience any complication due to the introduction of the microdialysis catheter. This opens possibilities for improved monitoring of the transplanted liver. The low Arg/Cit ratio during the first 4 postoperative hours in one of the patients that later needed retransplantation indicates that changes in the intra hepatic amino acids may reflect clinical outcome. This will be addressed in further studies. The disease is clinically progressive with organ dysfunction and is usually fatal about 10 years after the onset. TTR is mainly produced in the liver, and liver transplantation has been proposed an effective treatment for FAP. Living donor liver transplantation (LDLT) for such an inherited disease is not easy in donor selection, but has been a significant treatment in Japan due to the scarcity of cadaveric donors. We analyze the results of LDLT for FAP and evaluate the efficacy of LDLT on the progression of FAP. Methods: Since January 1999 to December 2003, seven patients with FAP, including 4 males and 3 females, have undergone LDLT at our center. Donors included 4 spouses, one mother and one daughter. If the donor was recipient's blood relation, the gene analysis was performed to exclude potential FAP. The recipients' age distribution was between 30 and 49 years old (median: 35). The median duration of symptomatic disease before transplantation was 9 months (ranged from 5 months to 8 years). The modified body mass index (BMI) was calculated to evaluate the change of nutritional system. The scoring of the disturbance in sensory, autonomic, motor and visceral nervous systems was performed and compared before and after transplantation. The follow-up periods ranged from 8 months to 4 years. Results: Three left lobes and 4 right lobes were used as the grafts and graft and recipient body weight ratio ranged from 0.47 to 1.18 % (median: 0.96 %). The pace maker was placed in two patients just before transplantation to avoid sudden cardiac arrest due to cardiomyopathy by amyloid accumulation. All recipients remain alive and have returned to normal social lives. The nutritional conditions have improved after transplantation in most patients. The scores of the disturbance in nervous systems have not changed significantly before and after transplantation, showing neither improvement nor progression of neurological symptoms. Conclusions: LDLT allowed the timely transplantation, and could prevent the progression of FAP. As the neurological symptoms presented preoperatively have not improved after transplantation, the earlier transplantation after the onset should be considered, if the door is available. Gene transfer and gene therapy techniques are being utilized to treat inborn errors of metabolism, hepatic cirrhosis and hepatocellular cancer. This is in keeping with the National Institutes of Health's recent proposals for support of translational research. This presentation aims to discuss the following proposed application of HGP to transplantation: • Histocompatibility testing: genome-based molecular typing • Interventions in the donor to make donor genome more compatible with the recipient • Modifying the donor organ to express 'compatible antigens' • Expansion of the genetic code to accommodate additional amino acids in strategic positions to increase compatibility • Molecular imaging of targeted macromolecules on donor organ and recipient immune system to diagnose early rejection and monitor allograft function • Pharmacogenetic or DNA diagnostic testing using microarray technology to develop 'genome-based' specific immunosuppressive therapy • Development of gene microarray technology to detect and monitor chimerism, evaluate genomic basis of transplant tolerance and develop genome-based tolerance assays • Unraveling of the human genome is expected to have a tremendous impact on the field of transplantation As the donor population becomes older, the quality of all donor allografts decreases. With decreasing numbers of ideal donors, one wonders if a donor is good enough to provide a pancreas and/or thoracic organs, should one assume that the liver will also be acceptable for transplantation? PURPOSE: To evaluate clinical outcomes in OLT recipients using grafts from liver-only procurements compared to multi-organ recoveries. METHODS: UNOS database was retrospectively reviewed for recovery and transplant outcomes of abdominal and thoracic organs between 1/99-12/02. The data were analyzed for OLT recipients with respect to: primary non-function (PNF) rate, 6month graft survival, and overall patient survival, with follow-up ending in June 2003. Four procurement groups underwent OLT: liver-only (L-O), liver-pancreas (L-P), liver-thoracic (L-T), and liver-pancreas-thoracic (L-P-T). RESULTS: There were a total of 20,334 OLTs performed in the time period. The overall rate of PNF was 3.6%, the 6-month liver allograft survival was 83.7% and patient survival was 77.9%. Following OLT, the PNF rate was significantly higher for grafts from L-O procurements compared to L-P and L-P-T procurements (4.4% vs. 2.4%, p<0.01 and 4.4% vs. 2.6%, p<0.01 respectively). No PNF differences were observed between L-O and L-T groups (4.4% vs. 3.8%, p=0.10). The 6-month liver allograft survival was superior in multi-organ recovery groups (L-P, L-T, L-P-T) compared to the L-O group: 84.8%, 84.6%, and 86.2% vs. 81.7% respectively, p<0.001 for all. Overall patient survival was also superior in L-P, L-T, and L-P-T groups compared to the L-O group: 80.2%, 79%, and 80.4% respectively vs. 75.6%, p<0.0001. CONCLUSION: Clinical outcomes are superior in OLT recipients when the graft is procured as part of a multi-organ recovery compared to liver-only recovery. In addition, recovery of the pancreas along with the liver provides a lower PNF rate for OLT recipients compared to liver-only or liver-thoracic procurements. These observations suggest that donors who are ideal enough to provide pancreatic allografts also provide the best hepatic allografts. Results: The IPITTR reviewed 296 cases of high-risk transplants performed using donors with known or incidentally discovered malignancies. 124 cases (42%) of donor transmission were confirmed. The majority were observed in kidney recipients (n=222), followed by liver (n=38), heart (n=27), lung (n=5) and pancreas (n=4). The incidence of donor malignancy transmission was uniform among allograft recipients, occurring in 45% of renal, 37% of liver, and 30% of cardiac. The majority of donors involved in this study were cadaveric (n=296); there were three living unrelated donors and the remainder were living-related donors. Mean donor age was 37.4 ± 15.0 years of age. Seventy-six (26%) of the donors had a known history of malignancy. Mean recipient age was 37 ± 15.4 years. The mean time from transplantation to presentation of the donor malignancy transmission was 2 months, with a range of 2 days to 38 months. The most commonly tumors encountered in eligible donors are central nervous system tumors. A recent study from the IPITTR evaluated risk factors involved in donor transmission. In this series, an overall CNS tumor transmission rate of 23% was observed, with several specific risk factors including high-grade tumors, ventriculoperitoneal and ventriculoatrial shunting, craniotomy, and external radiation having been identified. When no identifiable risk factors were identified, the incidence of donor transmission was only 7%. In the presence of one or more risk factors, the incidence of tumor transmission was increased to 46%. Two donor tumor histologies were noted with very high transmission rates: malignant melanoma and choriocarcinoma. Choriocarcinoma, a gynecologic malignancy, had an identified transmission rate of 93%, with a resulting 64% mortality rate. Malignant melanoma has demonstrated a 74% tumor transmission rate with a resulting 58% mortality. Utilization of organs from donors with a historic or active lung malignancy resulted in a 43% tumor transmission rate, with a corresponding 32% percent mortality rate. Conclusions: Donor transmission of malignacy is a real entity. While organs from donors with CNS malignancies can be used in the abscence of risk factors, the use of organs from patients with melanoma, choriocarcinoma or lung cancers should be avoided. Liver transplantation as a therapeutic method for treating liver terminal diseases causes a new problem after its implantation: the scarcity of organs. Objective: The aim of this study was to analyze, retrospectively, the donors classified as marginal and ideal and the relation of this classification with the survival of the recipients after orthotopic liver transplantation (OLT). Methods: A total of 197 OLTs were done in this service from 1991 to 2001. One hundred and forty eight became eligible for this study. The classification of marginal donors was based on criteria defined as follows. Major criteria: age over 55 years, aspartate aminotransferase greater than 150UI/l, serum bilirubin greater than 2mg/dl, serum sodium greater 150 mEq/l, use of dopamine greater than 10µg/kg/min or any other vasoactive amine, cardiac arrest, Intensive Care Unit (ICU) stays for more than 5 days and moderate or severe macrosteatosis. Minor criteria: dopamine usage bellow 10µg/kg/min, history of alcoholism, drug abuse, ICU stays less than 4 days, microsteatosis of any degree and mild macrosteatosis. Statistical analysis was carried out using Cox regression and the Kaplan-Meyer survival method. Results: The rate of marginal donors was 61.5%. In this study the 180 th postoperative day survival was 77.0%. Survival rates were 81.1% for marginal donor's recipients and 70.7% for ideal donor's recipient (p>0.05). Conclusion: The use of liver marginal donors was viable and could expand safely the numbers of liver transplants, diminishing the number of deaths on the waiting lists. Ringe 1 . 1 Department of Surgery, Center for Liver Disease, Drexel University College of Medicine, Philadelphia, PA, USA In the early days liver transplantation (LTx) was recommended in patients with advanced non-resectable hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC) resulting in poor long-term survival. Today, patient selection has become more stringent and is based on early, resectable cancer stages. However, present criteria used are different among transplant centers, which makes it difficult to compare results, and do not always accurately predict prognosis. We have reviewed the medical literature since 1954 to identify criteria published for the staging of HCC. To evaluate and compare the reliability of these criteria we focused on two questions: (1) Can the criteria be used in the decision making process before and for treatment? and (2) How accurately can these criteria predict prognosis? As a tool we used the certainty factor (C factor) as suggested by the UICC defining the level of diagnostic judgment and reliability: C1 clinical, C2 radiological, C3 biopsy, C4 resection, C5 autopsy. A total of twenty different HCC staging systems were selected and used for this compared analysis. • Eleven staging systems provided clinical and radiological assessment only. These systems were capable of selecting treatment without assessing a definite prognosis, e.g. the Milan criteria. • Two systems assessed pathological criteria only, e.g. Edmondson and Steiner. Both systems cannot be used to select therapy but provide prognostic information only. • Seven staging systems allowed to both assist in decision making for therapy based on clinical criteria as well as the assessment of a prognosis after surgery based on pathological data. An example is Vauthey. Ideally, staging criteria should be able to guide in therapeutic decision making and to predict long-term prognosis. However, accurate prediction of prognosis is usually based on pathological criteria, e.g. vascular invasion, which is usually not available before or without surgery. It would be desirable to use a uniform staging system in order to compare the results of different studies Roman Schumann 1 , Jeffrey T. Cooper 2 , W. Heinrich Wurm 1 , Richard B. Freeman 2 , Richard J. Rohrer 2 . 1 Dept. of Anesthesia; 2 Dept. of Surgery, Divison of Transplant Surgery, Tufts-New England Medical Center, Boston, MA, USA Introduction: The UNOS MELD score attempts fair allocation of scarce cadaveric donor livers, based on liver disease severity. Timely liver transplantation (LT) is also the treatment of choice for porto-pulmonary hypertension (POPH) and hepatopulmonary syndrome (HPS) from advanced,stable liver disease. However, afflicted patients may not meet MELD listing criteria and/or miss the opportunity for curative LT, once their expected morbidity and mortality from progressive POPH and HPS become prohibitive. Living donor (LD) LT is MELD independent, and we report successful longterm outcomes in one recipient with each disorder. Both patients' MELD scores were 11 (upper limit: 40). I. Porto-pulmonary hypertension. An echocardiogram (ECHO) in a 37 yo man with alcoholic cirrhosis showed a severely enlarged, hypokinetic right ventricle. Initial right heart catheterization results, and data after 32 months of epoprostenol (EPS) therapy (maximally 19 ng/kg/min limited by drug side effects) were: pulmonary artery pressure (PAP, mmHg) 110/70⇒44/20, pulmonary vascular resistance (dynes•sec•cm 5 ) 960⇒76, pulmonary capillary wedge pressure (mmHg) 14⇒15, and mixed venous oxygen saturation (%) 59⇒83. During right lobe LT from his sister, amrinone and inhaled nitric oxide were added to increasing EPS, then weaned postop. Graft unclamping was associated with a brief rise in PAP to 52/30. EPS was gradually discontinued over 7 months. At 24 months graft function is excellent, the patient works full time, and his POPH has resolved. II. Hepatopulmonary syndrome. Rapidly worsening HPS was diagnosed in a 49 yo woman with cirrhosis from Wilson's disease. Pulmonary function tests, an ECHO 'bubble study' (intrapulmonary shunts, normal cardiac function) and the following room air arterial blood gas values were diagnostic: pH 7.44, pCO 2 33, pO 2 47. Wheelchair bound from HPS, her exercise tolerance was minimal on 12 l/min home O 2 . Within 7 months after right lobe LT from her husband, she became wheelchair independent, intermittently requiring 4 l/ min of oxygen during ambulation. Conclusion: The MELD score does not adequately represent patients with life-limiting hepatopulmonary disorders. LDLT offers a timely, life-saving alternative in selected patients. Evidence-based prognostic recipient selection algorithms predicting acceptable LT outcomes are currently not available. Until then, careful individual risk/benefit analysis is mandatory. Ashok Jain 1 , Mark Orloff 1 , Kerrie Lansing 1 , Randeep Kashyap 1 , Mark Kelley 1 , Robert Betts 1 , Marilyn Menegus 1 , Adel Bozorgzadeh 1 . 1 Surgery, Strong Memorial Hospital, Rochester, NY, USA Introduction: CMV infection is the commonest viral infection after solid organ transplant transplantation leading to significant morbidity and mortality. Ganciclovir has been proven to be effective to prevent and treat CMV infection. Recently Valganciclovir (Valgan; Gannciclovir Hydrochloride) has been found to have significantly better absorption compared to gannciclovir (450 mg valgan absorption is equivalent to 3 gm oral ganciclovir absorption and 900 mg valgan equivalent to 5mg/kg intravenous ganciclovir) and has been suggested as prophylaxis for prevention of CMV infection after solid organ transplantation. Aim of the present study is to examine the efficacy of valgan prophylaxis after primary liver transplantation. Material Methods: Between July 2001 to May 2003, 203 consecutive post-liver transplant recipients (129 males, 74 females, mean age 53±11 years) received valgan prophylaxis (900 mg/day to 450 mg/every other day depending on the renal function.) for 3-6 months. All patients were followed for a minimum of 6 months. PCR positive CMV in peripheral blood diagnosed symptomatic CMV. [Routine periodic surveillance of blood PCR was not performed]. The rate of CMV infection was stratified according to donor and recipient status of CMV antibodies. GroupI= Donor +/Recipient +, Group II= Donor -/Recipient +, GroupIII= Donor +/Recipient -, and GroupIV= Donor -/Recipient -. Result: During the follow up period 29(14.3%) patients developed CMV (Positive by PCR in peripheral blood), of these 5 patients had invasive CMV on liver biopsies. The incidence was highest in Group III -14/54(25.9%) and lowest in Group IV-0/35. In Group I and II, it was 16.4%(12/73) and 7.3% (3/41) respectively. Mean time to symptomatic CMV infection was 169 ±117 (median174) days post transplantation. The rate of CMV for either donor or recipient positive status (Group I, Group II, and Group III) was 17.3% with an invasive CMV hepatitis rate of 3%. Conclusion: Overall rate of symptomatic CMV was 17.3% in either donor or recipients positive for CMV antibody despite high dose prophylaxis of valcyte, suggesting ineffectiveness of valcyte oral prophylaxis for prevention of symptomatic CMV in liver transplant recipients. Vijayaragavan Muralidharan 1 , Naoko Yoshimura 1 , David Mayer 1 , John Buckels 1 , Paul McMaster 1 , Darius Mirza 1 , Simon Bramhall 1 . 1 Liver Unit, Queen Elizabeth Hospital, Birmingham, United Kingdom Aims. In liver transplantation (LT), matching of the donor and recipient blood groups is important. Few studies have investigated the long term effects of non-identical but compatible LT in FHF, two of which have suggested an increased rate of acute and chronic rejection. We investigated the potential haematological effects and outcome in such LT recipients. Methods. Prospectively collected data on LT patients LT between 1984 and 2001 was analyzed. Mann-Whitney U-test was used for quantitative data, Pearson's chi-square test for categorical data, Kaplan-Meier method for actuarial survival and Wilcoxon method for comparison. Results. 220 patients underwent LT for FHF of which 161 received a blood group identical organ while 59 received a non-identical but compatible group organ. The identical blood group included 125 with group O, 48 with group A and 4 with group B. In the non identical group of patients there were 11 recipients with blood group AB (donor A=05; B=01; O=05), 36 with group A (donor group O) and 12 with group B (donor group O). Haemolytic anaemia was seen in 2 patients in the non identical group (3.4%) and one in the identical group (0.6%). Both patients in the non-identical group demonstrated graft vs host type antibodies relating to the haemolysis and required blood transfusions. Disseminated intravascular coagulation was also present in 4(6.8%) of the non-identical recipients. There was no difference in the number of patients requiring peri-operative transfusion (96.6% vs 98.1%) or in the volume of blood transfused (5 vs 6 units). There were no differences between the non identical and identical group with similar acute rejection rates (41% vs 43%,) 30-day graft loss (30.5% vs 24.2%), sepsis related deaths (14% vs 16%). 30-day survival (74.6% vs 78.3%) and 1 yr survival (60.6% vs 71.4%) were all comparable. Long term outcomes were comparable including 5 year patient (55% vs 63%, ns) and graft survival (51% vs 58%, ns) and graft loss from chronic rejection (3.4% vs 3.1%). A graft vs host type haemolytic reaction is a transient and uncommon complication (3.4%) in these patients. Blood transfusion in the presence of haemolysis should be undertaken with donor specific blood to prevent continued haemolysis. Long term outcomes are comparable to those with identical organs. Peter Faybik 1 , Gabriela Berlakovich 2 , Claus G. Krenn 1 , Amir Baker 1 , Heinz Steltzer 1 , Alois Werba 1 , Michael Zimpfer 1 , Hubert Hetz 1 . 1 Anaesthesiology and Critical Care Medicine, University Hospital, Vienna, Austria; 2 Transplant Surgery, University Hospital, Vienna, Austria Objective : Besides the shortage of liver grafts, contraindications for orthotopic liver transplantation (OLT) in some patients with acute liver failure (ALF) forced a development of liver support systems. A variety of liver support systems have been evaluated but none of these has yet proven to be entirely effective. We use the Molecular Absorbent Recirculating System (MARS) that supports the excretory and detoxification function of the liver. MARS utilises selective hemodiafiltration with countercurrent albumin dialysis aiming to selectively remove both water-soluble and albumin-bound toxins of the low and middle molecular weight range. MARS can clear toxins and mediators (IL-6, TNF-a) that are involved in remote organ dysfunction accompanying ALF and also increases albumin binding sites for free unbound fractions of these molecules and thus supports liver regeneration. Furthermore, additional factors thought to be essential for liver regeneration are not removed because of higher molecular weight. Thus MARS may support the regeneration of patient's native liver or bridge him until OLT. Methods : Four patients with acute liver failure were treated with MARS. The precipitating cause for ALF were liver resection (1), hemorrhagic schock with ischemic hepatitis (1) and acetaminophen intoxication (2) . All of them fulfilled the King's College Criteria for OLT but it was denied due to contraindications such as sepsis (3) and malignoma (1) . In one patient the liver biopsy revealed high likelihood for spontaneous regeneration. In all patients 1 to 5 MARS treatments were performed lasting for 6 -20 h. The effect of MARS treatments were evaluated using blood chemistry, coagulation tests and plasma disappearance rate of indocyanine green dye. Results : Three patients survived without OLT and one patient died (acetaminophen intoxication). In all cases OLT was considered to be necessary, but contraindicated and denied. However the patient who has died was already admitted in a very bad condition and the therapy was started later than in other cases. Conclusion : Our data suggest that MARS may be an alternative modality of treatment for patients who are contraindicated for OLT, patients who are waiting for the grafts or are likely to recover spontaneously. This may help to save liver grafts. Further randomised clinical trials are needed to warrant this therapy. Thomas 1 , Ann Cho 2 , Andrew Raheim 3 , Manish Gupta 1 , Todd Merchen 1 , Steve Rudich 1 , Micheal Hanaway 1 , E. Steve Woodle 1 , Joseph Buell 1 . 1 Division of Transplantation, University of Cincinnati College of Medicine, Cincinnati, OH, USA; 2 Radiology, University of Cincinnati School of Medicine, Cincinnati, OH, USA; 3 Pathology, University of Cincinnati School of Medicine, Cincinnati, OH, USA Primary liver malignancies (PLM) are the fifth most common cancer world wide and is the third leading cause of cancer-related deaths in the USA. The current standard of care for PLM is surgical resection. The minority of these patients will have acceptable morbidity and mortality. Liver transplantation (Ltx) in selected patients have produced acceptable outcomes. However, given recent data on tumor doubling rates and transplant waiting times, it is estimated that 25-45% of these patients will not be transplantable within 12 months of diagnosis. Ablative techniques, therefore, offer a solution for addressing this problem. We have hypothesized that radiofrequency ablation (RFA) of PLM is safe and produces acceptable outcomes. Referred patients were evaluated for liver tumor(s). All patients were diagnosed with a liver tumor(s) by either ultrasound (US), computed tomography (CT) or magnetic resonance imaging (MRI). Each patient was taken to the operating room for laparoscopic or open RFA. Each patient had pathologic verification of malignancy from core needle biopsies prior to RFA (60 intra-operative and 16 pre-operative). 76 patients were referred for evaluation of identified PLM and were acceptable for RFA. 57 patients (76%) were found to be cirrhotic. There were 143 lesions identified by preop radiological tests (2.07 lesions per patient) with an average size of 3.45 cm. 72 RFAs were completed laparoscopically and 4 through open laparotomies. At the time of RFA, intraoperative ultrasound (IOUS) was unable to identify 5 (3%) tumors. 82% of all tumors were found to be malignant on pathology. Of all operative biopsies (n=60), frozen section pathology identified 91.7% correctly. There were 2 false positive (3.3%) and 3 (5%) false negative diagnoses. Post RFA there were 9 complications and one recurrence of tumor prior to transplantation or death. There were 6 deaths (8%), 1 within 3 months after RFA from hepatic failure with associated hepatorenal syndrome. Of those patients who were transplanted (24), explanted tumor volumes were evaluated and found to be 30-99% necrotic. Radiofrequency ablation of liver tumors is a safe procedure, even in the cirrhotic patient. It can be used in adjunct to liver transplantation or as a stand alone treatment for those not Ltx candidates. Further study is needed to identify whether it is an acceptable option instead of Ltx. Gunnar Soderdahl 1 , Tomas Nordman 2 , Pehr Rissler 2 , Jerker Olsson 2 , Lennart Eriksson 2 , Bo-Goran Ericzon 1 . 1 Department of Transplantation Surgery, Karolinska University Hospital, Stockholm, Sweden; 2 Department of Microbiology, Pathology and Immunology, Karolinska University Hospital, Stockholm, Sweden Background. In order to improve the results after liver transplantation for hepatocellular cancer, adjuvant treatment with doxorubicine has been suggested. We have applied a model for evaluation of the effect of doxorubicine and/or cyclosporine A on liver tumor growth and antioxidant levels in the liver. Methods. Three chemically induced rat liver tumors of various differentiation were minced, suspended in saline and transferred to the spleen of 3 groups of syngenic rats. Each recipient group were then divided into 4 subgroups (A-D). Subgroup A received treatment with i.v.doxorubicine 0.5 mg/kg body weight once a week (n=10), subgroup B received i.p.cyclosporine 2.5 mg/kg body weight twice each week (n=10), whereas subgroup C received a combination of cyclosporine and doxorubicine (n=10). Subgroup D received no treatment (n=15). The rats were sacrificed after 5-22 weeks. Results. In the animals transplanted with a relatively high-differentiated tumor, no tumor cell proliferation was found in any of the subgroups. When the tumor exhibited a more pronounced dysplasia, doxorubicine significantly stimulated tumor growth. This effect was further increased by cyclosporine. In the animals that were transplanted with the most aggressive tumor, doxorubine inhibited tumor growth. When given together with cyclosporine, however, this inhibition was abolished. Cyclosporine alone had no effect on tumor growth. The levels of a-tocopherol, ubiquinol and ubiquinone in the liver were significantly increased by doxorubicine after 17 weeks of treatment. When combined with cyclosporine, the levels increased further, while cyclosporine alone had no effect. Conclusion. These data suggest that doxorubicine, especially when given together with cyclosporine, may have a stimulatory effect on liver tumor cell growth in rats. This effect correlates to a concomitant up-regulation of the antioxidative defence system in the liver. The calcinurin inhibitor, cyclosporine A (CsA), affects cancer growth and metastasis, but it is still unclear how cancer is progressed under general immunosuppressive therapy after organ transplantation. Herein we addressed how CsA promotes the dissemination of colon cancer cells that were transmitted to the liver. The metastatic cell line of rat colon cancer, RCN-H4, was originally developed from a Fisher rat, and stably transduced with firefly luciferase. First, 1 x 10 6 cells were then syngeneically inoculated into the liver of Fisher rats through the portal vein. The tumor-burden liver was transplanted into the Lewis rat. The animals were followed daily by 15 mg/Kg of CsA (p.o.). Tumor progression was evaluated by in vivo luciferase bioluminescent imaging. Substantial luciferase expression was observed in the grafted liver of the CsA-treated animals at day 15, but not in the graft of mock-treated animals. In the un-transplanted Fisher rats, this immunosuppressive regimen showed that tumor cells survived in the liver (nearly 100%) and metastasized to mesenteric lymph nodes (∼50%) at day 15. Luciferase expression was not observed in the lung, bone, and brain. Mock treatment with olive oil showed low survival frequency in the liver (17%). This tumor cell line expressed the chemokine receptors CXCR4 and CCR7, which have recently been implicated with organ-tropic metastasis and progression of tumors. Thus, these findings suggest that the immunosuppression with CsA allows the tumor cells in the donor graft to survive in the recipient, where some chemokine receptors may be mediated for tumor dissemination. Fatty change of partial liver graft has been a major concern in living donor liver transplantation due to its influence on graft size evaluation and posttransplant graft function. Hepatic steatosis is often associated with overweight, so we tried body weight reduction in potential living donors with fatty liver and/or obesity to alleviate hepatic steatosis. From January 1999 to December 2002, 447 healthy volunteers underwent liver donation for adult-to-adult living donor liver transplantation in our institution. Ultrasonography-guided per-cutaneous needle biopsy to the right liver has been carried out to nearly all living donors as a preoperative evaluation procedure for hepatic steatosis besides abdominal ultrasonography and triphasic computed tomogram. During the study period, there were 9 volunteers whose liver donation had been withheld due to histologic or radiologic findings of excessive hepatic steatosis and/or overweight. We had advised all of them to reduce their body weight. The goal of weight reduction by near 5% of initial body weight was achieved in 6 donors during 2-6 months, and mean proportion of weight reduction was 5.9±2.0% (3-9) of initial body weight. Their body mass index before and after weight reduction were 25.3±3.8 and 23.7±3.4, respectively. Total amount of hepatic steatosis was significantly changed from 48.9±25.6% before weight reduction and to 20.0±16.2% after weight reduction. The proportional change in microvesicular steatosis (from 30% to 5.6%) was more obvious than in macrovesicular fatty changes (from 18.9% to 14.4%). Six right lobe and 3 left lobe grafts were procured uneventfully from these 9 donors, and all 9 corresponding recipients survive more than 12 months to date. We think that short-term weight reduction of living donors will be helpful to alleviate excessive hepatic steatosis especially in microvesicular type and can contribute to expand the pool of marginal living donors. However, our data implicates that arbitrary body weight reduction can give erroneous information leading to underestimation of hepatic steatosis. Therefore, baseline assessment of hepatic steatosis should be mandatory before reduction of body weight to prevent its erroneous underestimation. Hiroyuki Furukawa 1 , Tsuyoshi Shimamura 2 , Maeng Bong Jin 1 , Tomomi Suzuki 3 , Masahiko Taniguchi 3 , Masahiro Hattori 3 , Toshiya Kamiyama 3 , Michiaki Matsushita 3 , Satoru Todo 3 . 1 Organ Transplantation and Regenerative Medicine, Hokkaido University School of Medicine, Sapporo, Japan; 2 Devision of Organ Transplantation, Hokkaido University Hospital, Sapporo, Japan; 3 General Surgery, Hokkaido University School of Medicine, Sapporo, Japan Objective: Under the scarce availability of cadaveric donors, living donor liver transplantation (LDLT) has become a standard therapeutic option for end stage liver disease in Japan. Risk factors affecting the outcome of LDLT has not been well defined. Reported herein is the analysis of the outcome and contributing risk factors. Methods: From September 1997 to December 2003 adult patients received LDLT. Recipient's age ranged from 21 to 61. Types of the grafts consisted of 44 right lobes and 32 left lobes. Major indications were postnecrotic cirrhosis (n=36) including 22 with hepatoma, cholestatic liver disease (n=21), and fulminant hepatic failure (n=16). Immunosuppression was with tacrolimus and steroid. Azathiopurine or mycophenolate mofetil was added if necessary. Contributing pre-, intra-and post-operative risk factors were analyzed for the survival rate, length of hospital stay, and postoperative peak total bilirubin (PTB) as endpoints. Results: Overall adult patient survival rates were 84.0% at 1 year and 76.3% at 5 year. With univariate analysis, risk factors affecting the survival rates were early fungal infection (p=0.0001), hepatic artery complication (HAC) (n=0.002), MELD score (0.016), recipient age (p=0.017), early bacterial infection (p=0.022), intraoperative platelets transfusion (p=0.035), and intraoperative FFP transfusion (p=0.050). With multivariate analysis, risk factors affecting survival rate were early fungal infection (p=0.004, Exp(B)95%: 2.5 to 27.8)), recipient age (p=0.04, Exp(B)95%: 1.7 to 19.8), MELD score (p=0.008, Exp(B)95%: 1.5 to 14.3), and intraoperative platelets transfusion (p=0.020, Exp(B)95%: 1.2 to 11.6). Significant factors for length of hospital stay were GV/SV ratio (P=0.007), HAC (p=0.010), postoperative apheresis (P=0.012), and venous complication (P=0.032). Significant factors for PTB were GV/SV ratio (p=0.0001), length of ICU stay (p=0.038), donor age (p=0.039) and preoperative total bilirubin (P=0.046). Conclusion: Survival and risk factors of LDLT are similar to cadaveric liver transplantation. Although small-for-size graft and elderly donors worsen the graft function, and venous complications prolong hospital stay in LDLT, those factors are not correlated with survival. To maximize the advantage of LDLT, it should be performed at optimal timing before the recipient's condition deteriorates. We will report on the development of software-assistants for the preoperative determination of functional liver volume in adult-to-adult living donor liver transplantation (LDLT). Moreover, we will present the methods and results of a worldwide internet based distant service in which these software-assistants are used to provide risk assessment and preoperative planning for transplantation centers. Evaluation of donors as well as surgical procedures vary from one transplant center to another, which adds to the challenges for a web-based distant service. Our distant service approach provides individual risk analysis and surgical planning information to the participating centers based on CT-scans from the donor. The information generated includes volumetric information for the total liver, the donor's individual vascular territories, and the functional liver volume, as well as anatomical details for all vascular systems. The software-assistants are in particular developed for the following tasks: -Determination and quantification of risks resulting from grafts or remnant lobes which have critical volume -Determination, quantification and visualization of risks resulting from grafts or remnant lobes which are left with compromised territories -Interactive Determination of appropriate cutting lines and their associated resulting risks The software has been used so far in the preoperative evaluation of more than 300 potential LDLT donors from 12 transplantation centers worldwide. Our clinical collaborators are from Germany, Italy, Japan, the Netherlands, Singapore, Taiwan, and the US. The web-based distant service has proven to be feasible to support planning in LDLT. Especially the determination of risks associated with different resection strategies is unique and considered very helpful by the clinical partners. Figure 1 shows the volume at risk of venous congestion in case of a resection right to the middle hepatic vein without anastomosis of the V5 branches. Currently, in adult-to-adult living-donor partial liver transplantation, homografts from cadavers or the portal vein, external iliac vein and others within the liver removed from the recipient are utilized to increase the inner diameter of the portal and hepatic veins and IVC or to expand the anastomotic site, but these techniques have the disadvantages of limited tissue availability and a complicated procedure for harvesting the vein. To solve these problems, we developed an easy-to-use, tissue engineered patch and assessed its applicability to the reconstruction of the portal and hepatic veins during living-donor liver transplantation. [Materials] A bioabsorbable polymer sheet was used as the patch, with no cells seeded. The sheet was designed to be absorbed in the body within 6 to 8 weeks. Hybrid pigs were laparotomized under general anesthesia and used as the recipients of the sheet produced by tissue engineering. A side clamp was placed on the IVC in the lower part of the liver, followed by the removal of a 3 x 2 cm portion of the IVC, which was then patched with the sheet. Three months after transplantation, the graft site was removed and subjected to gross and histologic examinations. [Results] All the pigs transplanted with the sheet survived until sacrificed for the examination of the graft site three months later. The pigs gained weight, with no evidence of postoperative IVC occlusion. The polymer transplanted onto the IVC was found to have been completely absorbed, and the graft site was morphologically similar to the native IVC on gross examination. The patched IVC was neither stenosed nor deformed. Immunohistology revealed that the patch site was covered with endothelial cells, with the smooth muscle observed in the media. Like the native IVC, the patch site tested positive for factor VIII. [Conclusions] This is the first report of successful patching of the IVC using a cell-free bioabsorbable polymer material. The results suggest that such a polymer sheet can be used for the reconstruction of the IVC, hepatic and portal veins during living-donor liver transplantation (Background and Aims) Mycophenolate mofetil (MMF) has been investigated as an effective agent to allow the reduction of dosage or discontinuation of calcineurin inhibitors in patients with significant impairment of renal function. However, the absorption of MMF through the gastrointestinal tract was not fully verified yet during the immediate posttransplant days. Therefore, the event of acute rejection should be intensively monitored during the period in those recipients with reduced or no use of calcineurin inhibitors. The purpose of this study is to determine the protocol of immnosuppressive therapy during immediate posttransplant period for the recipients with impaired renal function after living donor liver transplantation. (Methods) One hundred forty two cases of adult-to-adult living donor liver transplantation were performed in our institution between January 2003 and December 2003. Among them, there were 87 (61%) patients with impaired renal function. These 87 recipients were classified into 2 groups according to immunosuppressive regimen with the exception for steroid administration. Group 1 was administered only oral MMF over 5 days during the first 1 week (n=23), and group 2 was administered oral MMF plus low-dose calcineurin inhibitor (n=64). These two groups were followed up for detection of acute rejection. (Results) The overall incidence of acute rejection during the first year was 21% in group 1 and 4% in group 2, resulting significant difference (P=0.028). However, there were no statistically significant differences between these two groups in the aspects of age, sex, preoperative condition (serum creatinine level, MELD risk score, CTP score, UNOS status), and survival. (Conclusion) In living donor liver recipients with significant renal function impairment, the sole oral administration of MMF with steroid during immediate postoperative period seemed to be vulnerable to acute cellular rejection. Therefore, combined administration of low-dose calcineurin inhibitor in intravenous route and MMF in oral route is recommended in patients with renal function impairment after living donor liver transplantation. Around 50% of patients elegible for right lobe adult-to-adult living-donor liver transplantation (LDLT) are unable to identify a suitable living donor (LD). It has recently been shown that the proportion of Hispanic recipients with suitable LD was ≥2-fold greater compared with other ethnic groups. Having larger families, with more potential donors, and a greater predisposition to donate among Hispanics appear as the most likely explanations. Goal: To investigate the applicability of LDLT in a Hispanic transplant center. Methods: From 02/00 to 11/03, LDLT was offered to 78 adults. Following an interview with a transplant surgeon, all potential LD underwent a non-invasive evaluation including liver and blood tests, hepatitis serologies and doppler ultrasound (US) with volumetric assessment. Celiac angiography was obtained in all accepted LD, a few days before surgery. Liver biopsy was indicated in selected cases mostly when a single potential LD for certain recipients had abnormal liver tests, increased BMI or steatosis on US. Results: A suitable LD was identified for 61 of the 78 recipients (78%). Of the 78 LD evaluated for the 61 recipients, the 1 st LD was accepted in 48 (79%), the 2 nd in 9 (15%) and the 3 rd in 4. All 26 potential LD were found unsuitable for the remaining 17 recipients (22%). Overall, of the 104 LD evaluated, 61 were accepted (59%) and 43 rejected (41%): 9 (21%) on the initial screening due to age or small body size, 32 (74%) after the noninvasive evaluation (US-steatosis in 15, small liver size in 8, psychosocial issues in 4, vascular anatomy in 3 and for other reasons in 2) and 2 (5%) following angiography. Liver biopsy was performed in 11 LD (11%). Six were rejected due to severe steatosis associated with significant inflammation or fibrosis in 5 and hemosiderosis in 1. Histological examination of the 5 accepted LD showed mild to moderate steatosis (<50%) with minimal or no inflammatory changes or fibrosis. Among the 61 recipients with a suitable LD, 4 were transplanted with deceased donors, 3 developed contraindications (2 severely decompensated cirrhosis and 1 cancer progression), 1 remains listed and 53 (87%) underwent LDLT. Conclusions: 1) This study confirms the increased applicability of LDLT among Hispanics and 2) LD with mild histological abnormalities appear to fare well following LDLT although this should be confirmed in larger series 91 SEQUENTIAL LIVER AND KIDNEY TRANSPLANTATION FROM A LIVING-RELATED DONOR. Ichiro Koyama 1 , Shohei Fuchinoue 1 , Tamotsu Tojimbara 1 , Ichiro Nakajima 1 , Satoshi Teraoka 1 . 1 Department of Surgery, Kidney Center, Tokyo Women's Medical University, Tokyo, Japan Patients and Methods. Six cases of sequential liver-kidney transplantation (LTx, KTx) from living related donor were performed on five pediatric and one adult patient. Indications for LTx were primary hyperoxaluria (PH1, four), congenital hepatic fibrosis (CHF, one), and intrahepatic biliary congestion (IHBC, one). Kidney allografts were transplanted from 3 to 10 months after LTx from the same donor. Initial immunosuppressive regimen was consisted of tacrolimus or cyclosporine and corticosteroids. The steroid was withdrawn after KTx in all six patients. Results. With a mean follow-up of months, the overall patients survival rate was 83% (5/ 6). Case 3 recipient died due to rejection and bile leakage on days 202.In five recipients, steroid withdrawal was successfully accomplished after KTx. In case 1 recipient, no immunosuppressants were administered from 5 years post-KTx. Mycophenolate mofetil (MMF) alone has been administered to case 2 recipient instead of tacrolimus due to tacrolimus-induced nephrotoxicity.
