In the situation that cannot expect cadaveric doner such as in Japan, livingrelated liver and kidney transplantation is an attractive and appropriate alternative in patients with end stage renal disease and concurrent liver disorder. Multiple organ transplantation from the same donor may facilitate the induction of immunotolerance. Biliary complications are a major source of morbidity following LDLT. Variations in biliary anatomy pose a technical challenge for reconstruction. Up to a third of potential donors are rejected based on anatomic abnormalities. A single duct-to-duct anastomosis is performed whenever possible. This technique is preferred over hepaticojejunostomy as the former is more physiologic and provides access to the biliary tree for both diagnostic and therapeutic interventions. Some centers consider multiple ducts a contraindication to donation whereas others would generally construct a Roux-en-Y biliary-enteric anastamosis. However, multiple duct-to-duct anastomoses can be a safe and effective alternative. Methods: From September 1999 to August 2003, 71 adult LDLT were performed at our center. All donors had MRI and intra-operative cholangiograms to evaluate the biliary anatomy. As a result of early segmental branching 2 ducts had to be anastomosed in 9 recipients. Four patients had a Roux-en-Y hepaticojejunostomy and 5 patients had double duct-to-duct anastomoses performed. Of these, 3 patients had the donor's accessory right hepatic duct anastomosed to the recipient's cystic duct stump and the main right hepatic duct to the right or common hepatic duct. In the remaining two patients, the donor's two ducts were anastomosed to the right and left hepatic ducts. All anastomoses were performed using 6-0 or 7-0 PDS in a running fashion. T-tubes and stents were used in all cases. Results: Of the five patients with double duct-to-duct anastomoses, two patients developed strictures diagnosed two and seven months postoperatively. A third patient had an early postoperative anastomotic leak (7 days postop). The remaining 2 patients had uneventful postoperative courses. All complications were managed percutaneously and /or endoscopically without reoperation. Although the initial complication rate was 60%, after a mean follow up of 18.6 months all patients were alive, doing well, and free of biliary complications. Conclusion: Although the complication rate for double duct anastomoses is significantly higher than that reported for single duct reconstruction, most complications can be managed non-operatively. Ideally, donors with a single right hepatic duct system should be sought. However, the presence of multiple ducts should not be a contraindication to donation and does not necessarily require a Roux-en-Y hepaticojejunostomy. Multiple duct-to-duct reconstruction appears to be a safe alternative. Since November 1997 70 living related liver transplantations were performed using the right hepatic lobe . The details of hepatic blood supply and outflow in living donor were evaluated by ultrasonography and CT-scan with 3D-reconstruction preoperatively. All variants of right hepatic lobe arterial blood supply were appropriate. The right hepatic lobe in 8 donors was supplied by 2 arteries (from celiac artery and from mesenteric artery). Only in 1 of these cases the celiac branch was significant and demanded arterial reconstruction by end-to-end anastomosis between right hepatic artery and cystic artery from mesenteric branch before implantation. Bifurcation of portal vein with at least short trunk of the right portal branch was preferable in donor according to convenience of graft perfusion for cooling and conservation. In 5 cases of 2 separate right portal branches the separate perfusion of each branch was used. The portal reconstruction of these 5 grafts was successfully performed on the back-table using recipient's portal bifurcation preliminary removed during hepatectomy. In 57 living related liver donors we observed the single large right hepatic vein. All additional small hepatic veins from the right lobe in these cases were considered to be of no significance and were ligated. The hepaticocaval anastomosis was being performed almost side-to-side between the artificially performed wide opening in the right caval wall and widely dilated hepatic vein's opening of the graft. In 10 cases of 2 equal right hepatic veins of the graft the inferior right hepatic vein was implanted with end-to-side technique after the upper anastomosis had been performed. In 3 other cases 3 hepatico-caval anastomosis were performed with the same technique. In all cases good function of grafts was obtained. No vascular complications were observed. The survival of grafts and recipients is 97.1 percent. The variants of vascular reconstruction in recipient allow to use right hepatic lobe from living donor in all cases without technical contra-indications. Background/Aim Living donor liver transplantation (LDLT) is now being carried out worldwide to alleviate shortages of cadaveric organs. The requirements of widening its indications, especially in adult patients, have been emphasized. Using the right liver graft is a way of meeting the need for LDLT in adult patients, however, it can be harmful for the donor. Therefore, prior to adopting the right lobe LDLT as a standard technique, we designed the present study to investigate the potential advantages and limitations of the left lobe LDLT for adult or adolescent patients, from the standpoint of reducing the risk of living donor. Methods Between June 1990 and December 2003, two hundred and three patients underwent LDLT, at our institution. In 101 of these, the graft comprised the left liver with or without the left portion of the caudate lobe. The perihepatectomy profiles of the donors, significance of left liver grafts, postoperative courses of the donors and recipients, and survival of the recipients were investigated. Results All the donors recovered well and returned to a normal lifestyle. None required banked-blood transfusion or re-operation, and postoperative liver function tests had satisfactory results. The cold ischemic time for the graft was 128 ± 52 minutes. The graft volumes (GVs) ranged from 302 to 686 ml, and GV/standard liver volume (SV) ratios varied from 26 to 81%, at the time of transplantation. No patient developed immediate postoperative liver failure, despite 86% of the liver grafts had GV/SV ratios less than 50%. The graft survival rate of patients received small liver grafts (GV/SV<35%, n=17) was significant lower than that of patients transplanted large liver grafts (GV/SV<35%, n=84) (63% vs. 92% at 1-year, respectively, P=0.002). The overall patient survival rates were 87%, 83% and 81% at 1, 3, and 5 years, respectively. Conclusion LDLT using a left liver graft imposes potential postoperative complications (a small liver is more vulnerable to injury, and recipients of small grafts are at higher risk of fatal complications during recovery). The grafts, corresponded to equal to or more than 35% of the recipient's SV, have yielded favorable results in adult/adolescent LDLT, with minimal burden to the donors. Four patients had minor complications including urinary infection (2), abdominal wall hernia (1) and wound infection (1). It is performed immediately after the reconstruction of HA, after the closure the operation wound and after carried into intensive care unit. Subsequently protocol Doppler US was performed according to our algorithm twice a day for 2 weeks, once a day for next 1 week and once two days until hospital discharge. The waveform was divided into three categories, "Normal", "Small" and "Others". Serial protocol Doppler US was continued on the "Normal" waveform. "Small" waveform means normal pulse wave with low velocity. On the "Small" waveform without degeneration of liver function, existence of dehydration was evaluated and, if presents, corrected immediately. After that if small waveform changes to "Normal" waveform, serial protocol Doppler US was continued. The "Other" waveform involves dampened, faded or absent waveform. When "Other", "Small" waveform with liver dysfunction or "Small" waveform without recovery after correction of dehydration was detected, urgent angiography was performed. When the treatment was failed or impossible to do, urgent reanastomosis of HA was performed. We considered the relation between mortality of HAC and serial Doppler US. Result HAC was found in 25 cases (33.8%) of 74 adult LDLT. HAT was only one case (1.4%) HAS were 19 cases (25.7%). HAA were 5 cases (6.8%). All HAT and HAS were suspected by protocol US and confirmed by arteriography. 3 HAA were found in the postoperative bleeding. The number of death was 11/ 74 (14.9%) in HAC group. But the death concerning HAC was only 3cases (4.1%). Serial Doppler US indicates the RI in HAC group was significantly lower than the RI in non-HAC (P=0.041). Conclusion Serial monitoring arterial flow by Doppler US after LDLT is a useful tool for early detection, and urgent treatment of HAC, which allows low mortality and long graft survival. Bolus steroids are usually given prior to liver graft reperfusion, based on the assumption that steroids protect from ischemia-reperfusion injury (IRI). This assumption was recently challenged by rodent experiments demonstrating that steroids can aggravate this phenomenon. Liver transplantation (LTx) between identical twins gives a unique opportunity to study the natural production of anti-and pro-inflammatory cytokines during IRI in the complete absence of confounding effects due to alloimmune response and immunosuppression (IS), in particular steroids. Methods A 38-year-old male with HCV-related cirrhosis and multiple hepatocellular carcinomas received a hepatic right lobe graft from his identical twin. No IS (including no intraoperative bolus steroids) was administered. Total cold ischemia time was 276 minutes. IRI was assessed by transaminases. Serum was sequentially examined by ELISA for proinflammatory (IL-1β, TNF-α, IL-8) and regenerative / anti-inflammatory (IL-6, IL-10) cytokines. The peak levels of serum transaminases (ALT/AST; 511/550 IU/L) were low and comparable to those usually observed in our standard LTx patients receiving steroids (ALT/AST; 393.6±564.5 / 394.2±748.0 IU/L). IL-6 dramatically increased after removal of the native liver and rapidly reached its highest value 2 hours after reperfusion (400 times higher than baseline). IL-10 is also highly and rapidly augmented (peak value at 1hour after reperfusion, 40 times higher than baseline). However, IL-1β and TNF-α did not increase during and after LTx. IL-8 slowly increased during LTx but reached its highest value post Tx. In the absence of steroid, IL-6 (cytokine essential for liver regeneration) and IL-10 (potent anti-inflammatory cytokine) dramatically and rapidly increased during LTx while increase in IL-1β,TNF-αand IL-8 (typical pro-inflammatory cytokines) was less overt. Therefore, the predominant balance of protective cytokines during LTx may naturally contribute to protection of liver grafts from IRI. Thus, the concept that intraoperative administration of steroids is necessary to protect from IRI in LTx has to be revisited. ( In live donor liver transplantation (LDLTx) organ procurement is usually well controlled, allowing the assessment of liver preservation and early graft function under standardized conditions. Since there are not many publications on the use of histidine-tryptophanketoglutarate solution (HTK) we prospectively studied its efficacy and safety in LDLTx. Our consecutive series included 21 patients (19 adults, 2 children; age 9 mo -61 yr), suffering from end-stage cirrhosis (14), malignant liver tumors (6), and acute hepatic failure (1). All donors (age 22 -69 yr) had normal liver function tests, and included six cases with hepatic steatosis (5 -20 %. The donor operation was performed by the same surgeon, and resulted in 19 right lobes, one left lobe, and one left lateral graft (mean graft weight 837 ± 218 g, graft-recipient-body weight ratio 1.33 ± 0.74 %). Liver preservation was done in a standard fashion by gravity perfusion with HTK, starting in situ through the portal vein (4.6 ± 1.0 L), and continued on the backtable with infusion via the hepatic artery (1.0 ± 0.5 L) for 10 min each to allow for adequate equilibration. Aditionally, the bile ducts were flushed with HTK. The first warm ischemia time (WIT) was less than one minute, the second WIT was 37 ± 15 min, and the total ischemia time was 194 ± 69 min. There was no primary non-function in this series. All liver grafts showed good recovery with maximum aspartate aminotransferase 574 ± 519 U/L, total bilirubin 13.97 ± 4.77 mg/dL, and partial thromboplastin time 50.13 ± 9.83 sec, respectively. One graft was lost from parenchymal fracture secondary to portal hyperperfusion after six days, and the patient was salvaged with retransplantation. Five patients died 14 -111 d after surgery from various infectious complications, none of them related to impaired graft function. 7/21 recipients developed early biliary leakage within 8 -45 d from the resectional surface, and following multiple duct reconstructions. There was no late ischemic type biliary lesion. These results confirm earlier observations that HTK solution is effective and safe -also when used in LDLTx. Potential advantages of HTK in comparison to other preservation solutions are: low potassium concentration, low viscosity, no particles, in situ perfusion, no need to flush before reperfusion, improved biliary protection, better recovery of microcirculatory changes, ready to use, and lower costs. Since the risk-benefit ratio is of particular importance in LDLTx the use of HTK solution should be encouraged. PURPOSE: Target C-2h (C2) levels of Cyclosporine A (CsA) have not yet been well established for living donor liver transplantation (LDLT). The aim of this study was to establish optimal use of CsA using C2-monitoring for LDLT patients. METHODS: 19 patients (6 male and 13 female) were enrolled in this study. Mean age were 46 +/-14 years. 12 patients were assigned to the first protocol: CsA dosing was initiated at 10-15 mg/kg/day orally within 24 hours posttransplant. CsA dose was adjusted to get the target levels of C2: 800-1200 ng/ml (4 patients; 500-900ng/ml). At the same time C0 levels were monitored and maintained under 500 ng/ml to avoid side effects. On day 6, time-concentration curve was drawn with C0,2,4,6 and 12 levels. In the late 7 cases, the second step protocol was applied; 10 mg/kg/day CsA was fixed for initial 2 days (2 patients; for 10 days) by nasogastric or enterofeeding tube. Target C2 and C0 levels were 500-800 ng/ml (C2) and <350 ng/ml (C0), respectively. RESULTS: 2 out of 12 patients (16.7%) had acute rejection in the first protocol. However, the patients enrolled in this protocol tend to be overdosed because of unstable absorption (C0 = C2) or abrupt increase after malabsorption in the early postransplant period. Adverse events were observed 10/12 patients (83.3%): infection (3cases), oral ulcer (3), renal dysfunction (3), hypertension (2), and headache (1). In the second protocol, 2 out of 7 patients (28.6%) had acute rejection with low C2 levels. No severe adverse event was observed in this protocol. CONCLUSION: Clinical outcome was satisfactory but many LDLT patients displayed unstable absorption profiles in the early posttransplant period after LDLT. Further investigation might be required to establish an optimal use of CsA using C2-monitoring for LDLT patients. Objective: Hepatic arterial reconstruction is one of the most difficult procedures in living-donor liver transplantation. If hepatic artery thrombosis occurs after liver transplantation, the recipient clinical prognosis will be uneventful. The aim of this study was to summarize the experience of the microsurgical technique in the reconstruction of hepatic artery in living donor liver transplantation. Methods: thirty cases of LDLT have been performed in our Center from Jan 2001 to Dec, 2003. In 30 cases, left lobe with middle hepatic veins was harvested in 20 cases, left lateral lobe in 3 cases and right lobe in 7 cases. Depend on intra-operative ultrasound and preoperation evaluation, donor operation was determined on the anatomic analysis of hepatic veins, portal veins and hepatic artery. The branch of the recipient's hepatic artery needs to be dissected as peripherally as possible. Hepatic arterial reconstruction was performed using microsurgical technique with direct end-to-end anastomosis (recipient hepatic artery to graft hepatic artery) as long as possible with interrupted 8-0 Prolene sutures and double clip. Result: 5 grafts have two hepatic arterial branches. The hepatic arteries in 16 cases were less than 2mm in diameter. The range time required for an arterial reconstruction was 24-40min. Hepatic artery thrombosis was occurred in 2 cases (6%), both of which needed retransplantation. The microsurgical technique in reconstruction of hepatic artery has significantly decreased the incidence of hepatic artery thrombosis and has significantly improved the surgical outcome in living donor liver transplantation. Purpose: Right lobe donation is technically more difficult and need to define surgical technique and has more risk for surgical complication. Right lobe donation usually matched graft size but safety of donor is major concem. We reviewed our experience of donor hepatectomy using right lobe in regarding to safe of our donor operations, retrospectively. Methods: Retrospective analysis of 42 donor operations for adult LDLT using right lobe was performed. We observed the patient characteristics, the operative findings, liver regeneration, peak liver enzymes (AST, ALT, bilirubin) as donor risk, mortality and morbidity. Results: The peak value of liver enzymes in the group of less the 30% of remained liver were significantly higher than the group of more than 30% of remained liver and these values could induced the risk on donor. The postoperative peak value of liver enzymes were increased according to degree of fatty change especially in case of more than 10% fatty change even without significance. There was no donor mortality and most important complication was biliary injury, bile leakage and biliary stricture. Conclusion: Right lobectomy for donor operation requires a meticulous surgical technique to minimize donor morbidity. Right lobectomy can be performed safely with minimal risk in case of careful donor selection that the remained liver volume exceed 30% of the total liver volume and the liver of minimal fatty change. Purpose: The living donor liver transplantation (LDLT) has increased in the number because of the shortage of cadaveric liver graft. For the adult recipients, LDLT using right lobe could meet the metabolic demands of the adult recipient and so, is the most common procedure in our hospital. The management of venous drainage from segment 5 and 8 tributaries is in controversy. We reviewed the vascular techniques for middle hepatic vein reconstruction with patent rate and the effect on the early liver function recovery. Method and Result: From Jan. 1999 to Aug. 2003, 95 LDLTs using right lobe were performed and in 89 cases of LDLT, a venous conduit from middle hepatic tributaries was made. The autogenous saphenous vein was used in 85 cases, the cadaveric iliac vein in 3 cases and the autogenous umbilical vein in 1 case. The single autogenous saphenous veins were used for the conduit after a saline distension in 55 cases. Among them, 2 cases with a vein from segment 8 were anastomosed to the side of the saphenous vein sequentially. The reversed Y shaped autogenous saphenous vein branches were used for the multiple meddle hepatic vein tributaries drainage in 12 cases. The saphenous venoplasty were used for large sized middle hepatic vein in 15 cases. Seventy-seven cases were available for patent study on the 7th postoperative day by CT scan and in 40 cases (51.9%) out of 77 cases, the venous flow was patent and continued to be patent 3 months later. The postoperative changes in the liver function test (AST, ALT, bilirubin) were determined in both groups. In the 37 occlusion cases (especially in small for size graft), the mean AST, ALT, bilirubin level was higher than that of the patent group until the second week but this was no statistically significant. The graft regeneration activity of the occlusion cases on POD 7 th day by CT volumetry was also less compare to that of the patent cases. For early good graft function after LDLT, an initial physiological decongestion of the graft should be considered. More beneficial liver function and more regenerative activity in the early postoperative period were found in the patients with patent middle hepatic vein despite the high occlusion rate. Conclusion: Physiologic venous drainage procedure from the middle hapatic vein are recommended for rapid graft decongestion and an initial good graft function. This is a report on our strategy and results of living donor liver transplantation for hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC). It is most important to prevent graft recurrence for improving survival rate. Our strategy is based upon following three points: 1. Reducing the number of cancer cells in the peripheral blood as little as possible before transplantation. 2. Reducing the administration of immunosuppressant early in post operation for avoiding cancer cell attaching to the graft. 3. Prevention against cancer recurrence after transplantation. [Patients] From 1999 to 2003, eleven patients were transplanted due to HCC. Five patients satisfied Milan criteria but six exceeded. [Methods] Before transplantation fluorouracil and interferon beta were administered systemically as immunochemotherapy for reducing cancer cells in the peripheral blood. Human telomerase reverse transcriptase (h-TERT) mRNA by RT-PCR was used to estimate cancer cell levels in the blood. White blood cells of donor were infused intraportally during and after the operation so that immunosuppressants were reduced early after transplantation by inducing immunoligical tolerance achiving macrochimerism of CD56+ NKT cells in the graft liver. After transplantation fluorouracil was administered intraportally and cisplatin and epirubicin were systemically to prevent recurrence by the cancer cells arising from intraoperative manipulation. [Results] All three patients who were negative for h-TERT mRNA within Milan criteria were alive but one h-TERT mRNA positive patient was dead because of tumor recurrence. Two of six patients who exceeded Milan criteria were alive without recurrence, whose h-TERT mRNA changed from positive to negative before transplantation. [Conclusion] Blood h-TERT mRNA detection may be valuable for liver transplantation for HCC which exceeded Milan criteria. Yoshinobu Sato 1 , Hideki Nakatsuka 1 , Satoshi Yamamoto 1 , Toshiyuki Takeishi 1 , Takashi Kobayashi 1 , Kenichiro Hirano 1 , Takaoki Watanabe 1 , Katsuyoshi Hatakeyama 1 . 1 Division of Digestive and General Surgery, Niigata University Graduate School of Medical and Dental Sciences, Niigata, Niigata, Japan Aim of Study: Size mismatch is most obstacle in adult living related donor liver transplantation (ALRDLT). We have reported that acute elevated portal hypertension, reflecting wall shear stress against portal vein and sinusoid, become a trigger of liver regeneration following partial hepatectomy and excessive shear stress induce liver injury after massive hepatectomy. We have controlled portal pressure immediately after LRDLT below 25cmH2O by splenic arterial ligation, splenectomy, IMV-left renal venous shunting, and double outflow capacity(DOC) method of hepatic vein in left graft lobe. In this study, we investigated the relation between the posttransplant liver function and portal hypertension and the small for size graft in ALRDLT. Materials and Methods: Adults patients (n=16 to 68 years old) who underwent LRDLT from 1999, March to 2001, were divided into three groups, group I ; graft volume / recipient body weight (GV/RBW) ratio>1.0, group II; GV/RBW ratio<1.0. We analysed the relation between the GV/RBW ratio and the posttransplant serum total bilirubin, D/T ratio, COHb, and AKBR. Moreover we compaired the portal pressure and seurm T.Bil level between the traditional left hepatic venous reconstraction (n=11) and the DOC method (n=6) in left graft liver patients. Results: Serum T.Bil level peaked at 1 to 3 weeks and decreased thereafter. Hyperbilirubinemia after LRDLT wasa higher and more sustained among group II than group I patients(p>0.01) D/T ratios among group II recipients were significantly higher than those in group I (p<0.01) COHb and T.Bil were strongly correlated after LRDLT(y=0116x+1.275, r=0.810, p<0.0001) COHb among group II transplant patients were significantly higher than those in group I (p<0.05). Portal pressures after LRDLT and serum T.Bil levels in double outflow capacity method were better than those of traditional method. Conclusions: ALRDLT in cirrhotic patients using small-for-size grafts with excessive portal hypertension might lead to hyperbilirubinemia from the mechanism of Hemoxygenase-1 -CO system in micro hepatic circulation. The splenic arterial ligation and splenectomy, IMV-left renal vein shunting from the view point of inflow, and new hepatic venous reconstraction by DOC method from the view poin of outflow prevent from liver injury by a reduction of excessive portal hypertension after major hepatectomy. Oreste Cuomo 1 , Massimo Di Palma 1 , Lorenzo Iovine 1 , Walter Santaniello 1 , Renato Regine 2 , Alfonso Ragozzino 2 , Teresa Carfora 1 , Rosalia Anello 1 , Domenico Loffredo 1 , Marco Campoine 1 . 1 Laparoscopic, Hepatic Surgery and Liver Transplantation Unit, Cardarelli Hospital, Naples, Italy; 2 Radiology Unit, Cardarelli Hospital, Naples, Italy Selflimited liver hematoma can occur after iatrogenic liver injury. So far only 4 cases of hematoma following percoutaneous transhepatic procedure after liver transplantation have been reported, 3 out 4 treated by re-transplantation. We report a case of large expanding subcapsular hematoma in LDLT, successfully treated by cadaveric whole liver re-transplantation. CASE REPORT A 57 y.o. male, Child-Pugh C viral cirrhosis underwent LDLT. Estimated CT-scan right lobe graft volume was 845ml; actual graft weight was 574 gr.; GW/RW was 0.98%. Liver FNAB was performed 2 days before donor hepatectomy. At laparotomy a 2 cm subcapsular hematoma of segment 6, probably following liver biopsy, was observed. The right donor hepatic vein (HV) was small and anastomosed to the right recipient HV enlarged by incising the inferior vena cava (IVC) downward about 2 cm. and removing a piece of anterior wall. An accessory HV >5mm. was anastomosed to the IVC in endto-side fashion. Graft portal vein (PV) was anastomosed to the trunk of recipient PV; after reperfusion right PV flow was 1,6 l /mn. On p.o. day 1 US and CT scan revealed a large expanding subcapsular hematoma and the presence of hypodense spots in the graft compressed by hematoma. In the first 4 PODs doppler PV and hepatic artery velocity ranged respectively 71 to 66 cm/s. and 0,55 to 0,66 cm/s. On day 4 a second CT-scan was suggestive for the presence of blood clots around the liver. Liver function tests were stable. At laparotomy was only confirmed a large subcapsular hematoma, liver biopsy showed lobular spotty necrosis, limited hepatocytes balloning, cholestasis and ischemic cholangitis. Between day 7 and 9 liver and renal functions rapidly deteriorated (abundant ascites, elevated bilirubin, renal failure). A cadaveric whole liver retransplant was successfully performed. At histology massive subcapsular hematoma of segment 5, 6, 7 and ischemic damage of the liver graft were found. Discussion In our case a small right HV and the preservation of an HV >5mm probably failed to assure sufficient outflow. Furthermore high portal flow and rapid regenerative process of the partial graft may have caused rebleeding from the bioptic injury and the occurrence of a massive subcapsular hematoma, ultimately responsible, by compressing the congested liver, for hepatic failure necessiting retransplantation. Thus, LDLTx has been established as a therapeutic modality for patients with end-stage-liver-disease. However all of the patients cannot receive this procedure due to various reasons. In this study, we determine major obstacles for patients who were referred to our hospital but could not undergo liver transplantation (LTx). (Case Material)From September 1997 until December 2003, a total of 352 patients with acute or chronic end-stage-liver-disease were referred to our center for possible LTx. Forty six were children (with mean age 5 y.o., range 10 days to 17 y.o.) and 306 were adults (with mean age 48 y.o., range 18 to 73 y.o.). A hundred-nine patients (26 children and 77 adults) received LDLTx, 6 patients (all adults) received cadaveric liver transplantations (CLTx). (Result)Of three hundred fifty two referrals, 166 (47%) were not evaluated because of no necessity in 44 (13%), exceeding indication in 41 (12%), refusal from the patient or his family in 40 (11%), no living donors in 28 (8%), and financial matter in 11 (3%). The rest of 186 (53%) were evaluated, and 164 were medically indicated for liver transplantation; 109 had either LDLTx or CLTx, 24 were on the list, 24 died while waiting, 4 abandoned from financial matter, and 3 refused. Of overall 249 cases who did not undergo LDLTx, 67 (27%) had no living donors, 56 (22%) exceeded indication, 48 (19%) were medically unnecessary, and 10 (16%) refused from the himself or his family. (Conclusion)In the era of LDLTx, early referral is mandatory to save more patients with end-stage-liver-disease. Federica Dondero 1 , Camille Taille 2 , Daniele Sommacale 1 , Francois Durand 1 , Claire Francoz 1 , Alain Sauvanet 1 , Olivier Farges 1 , Herve Mal 2 , Jacques Belghiti 1 . 1 Hepatobiliary and Transplantation, Hospital Beaujon, Clichy, France; 2 Pneumology Introduction : The safety of donors is a crucial condition for living donor liver transplantation. In order to promote the report and analysis of donor complications, the aim of this study was to evaluate the incidence and severity of postoperative respiratory complications in 112 consecutive liver living donors (LLD). Patients and methods: From 1996, 112 LLD including 44 with right hepatectomy (RH) were analyzed through a prospective database. All respiratory events were assessed through clinical, bacteriological and radiological examination including a systematic ct-scan at day-7 in the last 59 ( 53%) and analyzed according to surgical procedures. Results : Respiratory complications were observed in 21 donors (19%). Major complications were present in 13 donors (12%) including pulmonary embolism in 7 (6%), bacterial pneumonitis in 3 (2.7%) and septic pleuritis in 3 (2.7%). Minor complications were present in 8 cases (7%) including iatrogenic pneumothorax in 3 (2.6%) and symptomatic pleural effusion in 5 (4.5%) requiring thoracocentesis in two cases. Pulmonary embolism in all cases was not severe and associated with deep vein thrombosis in only 4 cases. The systematic CT scan at postoperative days 7 revealed the presence of pleural effusion in 38 cases (95%) after right hepatectomy and in 9 cases (47%) after left hepatectomy. Pleural effusion was unilateral right in 55% of cases and bilateral in 45%. The alveolar condensations was present in 5 of cases (8%). In the subgroup with CT scan, chest x ray revealed pleural effusion in 41% (n=24) and alveolar condensations in 5% (n=3). The rate of major pulmonary complications was significantly higher after RH as compared to left procedure (27% vs 5%) (p<0.05). Patient age, sex, tobacco abuse, body mass index, intraoperative blood loss and abdominal complications were not correlated with the presence of respiratory complications. Conclusion : This experience of major surgery in healthy donors revealed that respiratory complications are more frequent than expected in early postoperative course. These respiratory complications, mainly observed after right hepatectomy, are underestimated by clinical examination and chest radiograph and not related to abdominal complication and medical history. Koji Yamamoto 1 , Yasutugu Takada 1 , Yasuhiro Fujimoto 1 , Hironori Haga 1 , Hiroto Egawa 1 , Koichi Tanaka 1 . 1 Department of Transplantation and Immunology, Kyoto University, Kyoto, Kyoto, Japan Background and Aim: We experienced first donor death in Japan in 2002. The liver biopsy showed that the donor had been suffering from NASH. There might have been NASH donors included in the donor pool in our program. To secure donor safety, we should understand the prevalence of NAFL and NASH in the donor population. Methods: We did retrospective review of liver zero biopsies for 263 right lobe donors from February 1998 to April 2003 (male 132, female 131, mean age 41.9 years old ). 1) The evaluation was made in terms of the extent of fatty change by hematoxylin and eosin staining. The samples were also scored based on histopathologic abnormalities in NASH from American Association for the Study of Liver Disease. 2) The postoperative laboratory data were compared based on the severity of the fatty change. Results: Among 263 donors, 143 donors (54.5%) did not show fatty change. Macrovesicular fatty change over 5% was observed in 47 donors (17.9%) and fatty change over 30% were seen with 4 donors (1.5%). Three of them were shown to have NASH, which led to donor death with one donor. The remaining 2 donors did not show any postoperative complications or abnormal laboratory data. The NASH score was higher with the dead donor (8 points) compared with other 2 NASH donors (5points, 5points). The peak AST and ALT tends to be higher with donors whose liver showed fatty change of 5% or more. Surviving NASH donors did not show any statistical difference compared with non-NASH donors in terms of postoperative laboratory data. Conclusion: There was no significant difference with the postoperative course and laboratory data as long as the fatty change remained around 30%. There may be a higher risk with advanced NASH donors. Background/aims: It is accepted that steatosis may compromise early liver graft function. However, the impact of steatosis in the long term has not been clearly investigated. The aims of this study were (a) to investigate the long term outcome of steatotic grafts as compared with non steatotic grafts or grafts with mild steatosis and (b) to determine whether initial steatosis in the donor represents by itself a risk factor for persistent steatosis or other delayed graft lesions. Patients and methods: 114 consecutive patients (71 males and 43 females) who all survived more than one year after liver transplantation (LT) were studied. 30 patients (26%) received a graft with more than 30% macrovesicular steatosis (Group 1) while the remaining 84 (74%) received a graft with less than 30% or no steatosis (Group 2). All had a biopsy at the time of reperfusion and one year after transplantation. Results: Indications for LT, age and gender were comparable in the 2 groups. Body mass index (BMI) was significantly higher in Group 1 than in Group 2 (26 ± 6 vs 24 ± 3, p=0.008). One year after LT, only 19 patients (17%) still had steatosis over 30%. Predictive factors for steatosis over 30% at one year were high BMI (29 ± 5 vs 24 ± 5, p=0.001), the increase in body weight during the first year (8 ± 13 vs 1 ± 9 kg, p=0.008) and dyslipidemia (20% vs 3%, p=0.04). Initial graft steatosis (Group 1 vs Group 2), alcohol consumption (10% vs 6%), diabetes (37% vs 20%) and HCV infection (42% vs 24%) did not have a significant impact on liver graft steatosis at one year. Paradoxically, one year after LT, the presence of fibrosis at any grade was significantly less frequent in Group 1 than in Group 2 (17% vs 40%, p=0.02). In patients with HCV infection, neither initial steatosis nor steatosis at one year had a significant impact on fibrosis. Among patients with one or more recognized risk factor for steatosis only 24% did have steatosis at one year. The results of this study suggest (a) that liver graft steatosis one year after LT essentially depends on recipient-related factors and not to initial steatosis in the donor, (b) that only 24% of those with risk factors for steatotis eventually develop steatosis at one year and (c) that initial steatosis does not seem to favor the development of fibrosis in either HCV-infected or non HCV-infected patients. Garcia-Valdecasas 1 . 1 Liver Surgery and Transplantation Unit, Hospital Clinic, Barcelona, Barcelona, Spain Introduction: Living donor right liver resection is associated with a significant morbidity and a not so negligible mortality. It was initially suggested that the overall risk should be similar to that of major elective liver surgery, however recent reports suggest that this is not so. The aim of this study is to compare the morbidity and mortality associated to right lobe living donor liver resections with that of right hepatectomies performed for metastatic disease. Materials and methods: Prospective data from 38 living donors who underwent right hepatectomy between March 2000 and June 2003 (living donor liver resection group, LDLRG) were compared to retrospectively collected data from 38 right hepatectomies performed for metastatic disease (metastatic liver resection group, MLRG) during approximately the same time period. We have analyzed demographic data (age, sex), intraoperative variables (blood loss, need for blood transfusion), postoperative liver function, as well as the morbidity associated with the procedure (bile leakage, biloma, intraabdominal abscess, wound infection, liver failure, etc) or not directly related to it (infection, pneumonia, pulmonary embolism). Results: Patients in the LDLRG were significantly younger (32±8 vs. 63±10 p< 0.001). Blood losses were similar (713±89 vs. 707±615), as well as the need for blood transfusion. Immediate postoperative liver function was similar in both groups. The overall incidence of complications was higher in the LDLRG (44.7% vs. 23.7%, p=0.09); surgically related complications were also higher in the LDLR group, reaching statistical significance in the incidence of bile leaks (8 vs. 1, p=0.02). However when non-surgically related complications were considered, the incidence was not different. No patient died in the LDLRG, while two patients did so in the MLRG. Conclusion: Living donor liver resection is a more demanding surgical procedure, associated to a higher incidence of surgically related complications, mainly due to the biliary tract. Although the role of routine abdominal drainage after liver resection for tumors has been questioned, abdominal drainage after donor right hepatectomy for live donor liver transplantation (LDLT) has been a routine practice in most transplant centers. Postoperative bile leakage has been reported to occur in 10% to 20% of the donors and abdominal drainage is considered mandatory. The aim of the present study was to evaluate the safety of donor right hepatectomy for LDLT without abdominal drainage. A prospective study was performed on 100 consecutive liver donors who underwent right hepatectomy for LDLT from July 2000 to September 2003. Biliary anatomy was carefully studied with intraoperative cholangiography using fluoroscopy. The middle hepatic vein was included in the graft in all except one patient. Parenchymal transection was performed using an ultrasonic dissector without vascular inflow occlusion. Right hepatic duct was transected at hilum and the stump was closed with 6-O polydioxanone continuous suture. Absence of bile leakage was confirmed with methylene blue solution instilled through the cystic duct stump. Abdomen was closed after careful hemostasis without drainage in all donors. There were 40 male and 60 female donors with a median age of 36 years (range, 18 -56 years). The median operative blood loss and operating time of the donors was 350ml (range, 42 -1400 ml) and 448min (range, 310 -640min), respectively. None of the donors required any blood or blood product transfusion. The median postoperative peak total serum bilirubin level was 59ummol/L, and peak prothrombin time was 17.9 seconds. There was no operative mortality. Median postoperative hospital stay was 8 days (range, 5 -30 days). Postoperative morbidity occurred in 19 (19%) patients, most of which were minor complications. No donor experienced bile leakage, intraabdominal bleeding or collection. None of them required surgical, radiological, or endoscopic intervention for postoperative complication, except for one donor who developed biliary stricture that required endoscopic dilatation. All donors were well with a median follow-up of 28 months (range, 3 -42 months). With detailed study of the biliary anatomy and meticulous surgical technique, donor right hepatectomy can be safely performed without abdominal drainage. Abdominal drainage is not a mandatory procedure after donor hepatectomy in LDLT. The problem of small-for-size graft has been inevitable in many of adult-to-adult living donor liver transplantation, especially,in case of using the left lobe graft. Procurement of the left lobe with caudate lobe was originated by the effort to increase the graft mass itself by 5%. However, there have been some arguments about the effectiveness of concomitant caudate lobe transplantation. We investigated the effort-effectiveness of concomitant caudate lobe transplantation and fate of the implanted caudate lobe. From January 2002 to December 2003, 290 cases of living donor liver transplantation (LDLT) were performed at our institution. There was 28 case of adult-to-adult LDLT using the left lobe with caudate lobe among them. The volumes of left lobe and caudate lobe were measured separately using computed tomogram (CT) volumetry. The operation time for concomitant caudate lobe procurement took average 30 minutes additionally over routine left lobe procurement because of dissection of the retrohepatic vena cava. We divided the left hepatic duct near the hepatic confluence not to make separate caudate duct after accurate localization with radio-opaque marker attachment. There was no separate caudate duct in 28 grafts in this series. The graft weight of left lobe with caudate lobe was 454±55 gm, yielding mean graft-recipient weight ratio 0.79±0.11. Volumetry of preoperative donor CT revealed that the volume of the caudate lobe was 24.1±6 mL, and it was equivalent to 5% of the graft volume. We had prepared the largest caudate vein with vena caval patch in 23 donors and reconstructed it in 22 recipients. The mean diameter of caudate vein was near 3 mm. In rest 5 donors, it was closed because of its small size of less than 2 mm. The implanted caudate lobe regenerated at lower rate after 1 month comparing with the regeneration of the left lobe. The caudate lobes in the group of caudate vein ligation revealed nearly no regeneration (from 22.5±7.8 mL to 22.0±6.7 mL) after 1 month comparing with the reconstruction group ( from 26.1±7.1 mL to 30.3±9.2 mL). We think that 5% increase of graft mass deserves to perform concomitant caudate lobe procurement at the expense of 30 minutes' effort. As most parts of the caudate lobe revealed normal perfusion on weekly follow-up using triphasic dynamic CT when the caudate vein was reconstructed, it seems to be reasonable to reconstruct one largest caudate vein. Features of the recipients with donors over 69 were similar to the general group. ICU median stay and hospital median stay after LT were 3.5 days (r 2-59) and 20 days (r 10-101) respectively. There were 61 infection episodes (70% bacterial) in 24 patients (58.5%). 8 patients suffered from CMV infection (20%) and 7 had a fungal infection (17%). Acute cellular rejection was diagnosed in 12 patients (29%). Arterial complications were found in 3 patients (7.3%). Biliary complications were seen in 5 patients (12.1%): 3 perikehr leakages, one stenosis and one hepatico-yeyunostomy dehiscence. There were 3 retransplantations: one acute (arterial thrombosis) and 2 late (HCV recurrence). Hospital mortality was 4.8% due to sepsis and cardiac failure. All these results do not reach statistical significance when compared to general group. Median follow up was 1054 days (r 21-2665). Patient survival at 1, 3 and 5 years in >70 years group vs. < 70 years was: 87% vs. 91%; 83% vs. 83% and 75% vs. 77% (p= n.s.). Graft survival comparing both groups was: 85% vs. 87%; 72% vs. 79% and 65% vs. 74% (p= n.s.). Survival to 1, 3 and 5 years of HCV patients receiving organs from donors > 70 years was not statistically different from the ones with younger donors : 81% vs. 83%, 81% vs. 73% and 68% vs. 70% (p= n.s.). CONCLUSIONS: Results obtained using livers from donors aged 70 and over are not different from younger donors. We think our results are based on good liver function tests, no severe steatosis and short cold ischemia time when using aged livers. Age should not be considered a major criterion when rejecting donors for liver transplantation. Methods: From the Studies of Liver Transplantation (SPLIT) a consortium of 39 pediatric LT centers in the US and Canada, we describe donor characteristics for 1,378 recipients of first liver only grafts, and examine in univariate and multivariate analyses their effect on patient (pt) and graft survival. Results: 51.8% of pts received a cadaveric donor (CD) whole organ, 28.0% a reduced or split graft and 17% a living donor (LD) graft. Marco Spada 1 , Matteo Cescon 2 , Alessandro Aluffi 2 , Michela Guizzetti 2 , Marco Zambelli 2 , Domenico Pinelli 2 , Monica Altobelli 3 , Giuliano Torre 3 , Bruno Gridelli 1 , Michele Colledan 2 . 1 ISMETT, UPMC, Italy, Palermo, Italy; 2 General and Transplantation Surgery, Ospedali Riuniti, Bergamo, Italy; 3 Pediatrics, Ospedali Riuniti, Bergamo, Italy Purpose: Although split liver transplantation (SLT) for pediatric patients is increasingly accepted, few data are available on graft size mismatch impact on the outcome. Methods: 184 cases of pediatric isolate, liver transplantations were prospectively evaluated for graft size matching. Graft weight was available in 157 cases (85%), of which 132 (84%) were split livers (122 left lateral segments grafts [LLS], 7 extended right grafts [ERG] , and 3 left lobes [LL]), and 25 (16%) whole livers. Seventeen cases (11%) were urgent cases. Patients were categorized in four groups by graft-to-recipient weight ratio (GRWR): small grafts (SG; GRWR ≤ 1.5%, 13 cases), medium grafts (MG; 1.5% < GRWR ≤ 3%, 42 cases), large grafts (LG; 3% < GRWR ≤ 5%, 72 cases), and extra large grafts (XLG; GRWR > 5%, 30 cases). Results: SG were associated with larger and older recipients, and with older donors; LG and XLG were more used in urgent cases. Post-transplant bilirubine clearance was delayed in SG. Post-transplant surgical complications were more frequent in SG, while no differences were observed in vascular, biliary and infectious complications and acute rejection rate among groups. When considering LLS only, unrelated death-censored graft survival in SG (actuarial 67% at 4 years) and XLG (75% at 4 years) was significantly lower compared to that in LG (93%), but not in MG (84%), while differences were not significant considering elective cases (75%, 83%, 92%, and 84% at 4 years in SG, MG, LG, and XLG, respectively). Patient survivals were not significantly different among groups (79%, 83%, 91%, and 87% at 1 year in SG, MG, LG, and XLG, respectively). A strong relationship was observed between GRWR and donor-to-recipient weight ratio (DRWR) (r 2 =0.15 for all grafts and r 2 = 0.47 for LLS). Conclusions: Pediatric SLT can be safely performed with a wide range of GRWR. GRWR can be fairly predicted by means of DRWR. The use of SG led to an higher incidence of surgical complications, while the use of SG and XLG resulted in a significant lower graft survival when considering LLS only. The analysis of elective cases showed that SG and XLG can be used obtaining results comparable to those obtained with MG and LG. Options for the treatment of early hepatic artery thrombosis (HAT) after liver transplantation (LT) are known to be the surgical or angiographic arterial revascularization and/or retransplantation (RET). In a retrospective study, our aim was to verify if recanalization can be really an option and a solution to early HAT or if it must be considered only a bridge to RET. Patients and methods Since 1983 through January 2004, 603 LT in 531 adult and pediatric patients (pts) were performed in our unit. We retrospectively reviewed pts whose grafts underwent early HAT (occurring after a time < 30 days from LT) and the treatments performed, with a mean follow-up of 3,9±4,2 years. Revascularization consisted of a surgical thrombectomy, with post-operatively intravenous heparin-based anticoagulable pharmacotherapy. We reviewed HAT who underwent arterial recanalization or/and RET, and the outcome of these procedures. Results 24 (3,9%) cadaveric liver grafts in 21 pts underwent 24 HAT; 9 (1,4%) early HAT occurred in 9 liver grafts -6 whole grafts, 2 left lateral segments and 1 "right liver"transplanted in 2 pediatric and 7 adult pts. Mean time of onset of early HAT was 12±7 days from LT. 5 pts underwent diagnosis of early HAT and prompt recanalization after a mean time of 10±6 days from LT, and subsequent RET 142±254 days from 1 st LT. More precisely 1 pt underwent RET 596 days after 1 st LT, whereas the other 4 pts after a mean time of 29±26 days from 1 st LT. Two pts underwent RET without any previous recanalization 12 and 20 days respectively after 1 st LT; one other pt underwent arterial recanalization 12 days after LT. One last pt was treated conservatively. In all cases, thrombectomy was effective with restoration of arterial flow only for a very short time after surgery, then HAT recurred. RET was only dilayed. 1-5-10 years graft actuarial survival of RET for early HAT was 68%. Discussion and conclusion Early HAT after LT occurred in 9 -1,4%-out of 603 transplanted grafts in 531 adult and pediatric pts. Early revascularization of hepatic artery was never successful, HAT constantly recurred, so RET was performed whenever a graft was available. According to our review, thrombectomy cannot be considered a reliable option for the treatment of early HAT. Only early RET is a reliable option for patient recovery. Background and Aims: Diagnosis of delayed vascular complications (DVC) which occur later than 3 months after liver transplantation (LT) is difficult to be made and DVC are often overlooked. DVC contribute to morbidity and mortality in LT. Purpose of this study is to investigate proper diagnosis and management of DVC. Patients and Methods: Live donor LT has been performed in 120 pediatric patients under 18 years at The University of Tokyo and Jichi Medical School. DVC was observed in 9(7.5%) patients. DVC consisted of hepatic vein stenosis in 6(5%) and portal vein stenosis in 3(2.5%). Hepatic vein stenosis occurred from 2 months to 7 years, so as portal vein stenosis did from 6 months to one year after LT. Clinical course, liver function of blood chemistry, Doppler US, computed tomography(CT), managements and outcomes have been investigated. Results: 1) Hepatic vein stenosis; Sever hypoalbuminemia, systemic edema and hepatosplenomegaly were observed in all of the patients. Serum transaminase level elevated slightly in five. Doppler US showed low flow velocity, dilatation of the hepatic vein and scarce positive component in the wave form. CT showed ascitis and hepatosplenomegaly. Trans-jugular interventional hepatic venoplasty was performed in two and per-cutaneous trans-hepatic venoplasty was done in four. Graft failure arose in three patients and two of them died two to three months after intervention. One is surviving for 18 months uneventfully after re-transplantation. Three other patients are surviving uneventfully for 2 months to 5 years after intervention without recurrence of stenosis. Melena and splenomegaly were identified in all of the patients. Liver function of the blood chemistry was within normal range. Doppler US showed slow portal velocity and splenomegaly which was also observed with CT. Percutaneous transhepatic interventional portal venoplasty was performed in all of the three patients. Two of them are surviving uneventfully for 3 to 6 months. Due to graft failure, retransplantation was done 3 months after venoplasty in one patient who is surviving without any complications after retransplantation. Conclusions: 1) Hypoalbuminemia, ascitis, melena and splenomegaly are early signs for the diagnosis of DVC. 2) Doppler US is a useful technique for making an early diagnosis of DVC. 3) Interventional venoplasty is useful management of DVC. BACKGROUND: Improvement of rejection after intestinal transplantation (ITx) led to the use of a steroid free protocol based on rATG preconditioning and induction with low dose tacrolimus (TAC). METHODS: 36 children 5 months to 20 years received pre-transplant rATG 2-3mg/kg, followed by ITx (liver/intestine n=12, intestine n=15, multivisceral n=6, modified/ multivisceral n=1, liver/intestine/pancreas n=1, intestine/pancreas n=1). Saiho Ko 1 , Ichiro Tanaka 2 , Hiromichi Kanehiro 1 , Hideki Kanokogi 1 , Junji Okayama 1 , Midori Shima 2 , Akira Yoshioka 2 , Alan Giles 3 , Yoshiyuki Nakajima 1 . 1 Department of Surgery, Nara Medical University, Kashihara, Nara, Japan; 2 Deapartment of Pediatrics, Nara Medical University, Kashihara, Nara, Japan; 3 Department of Pathology, Queen's University, Kingston, ON, Canada The cause of hemophilia is deficiency of coagulation factor VIII production in the liver, which can be cured by transplantation of non-hemophilic liver. Because the liver function of hemophilia patients is quite normal except for production of factor VIII, auxiliary partial orthotopic liver transplantation (APOLT) has a benefit that patient survival is secured by preserving native liver even in case of graft loss. However, it has not been known if the production of factor VIII from the graft of APOLT would be enough to prevent bleeding in patients with hemophilia. Methods. The feasibility of APOLT as a curative treatment for hemophilia was evaluated using a beagle dog with hemophilia A. Left lobe partial liver graft was taken from a normal beagle dog and orthotopically transplanted to the recipient with hemophilia A (factor VIII < 5%) after resection of the native left lobe preserving right lobe. Canine factor VIII was supplemented intravenously to the recipient until day 1 after transplantation to control operative bleeding. Tacrolimus and steroid were administrated as immunosuppressive therapy after transplantation. Results. The blood factor VIII activity increased to more than 30% immediately after APOLT, and maintained until the recipient's death of pneumonia on day 46. No episode of bleeding was seen during observation. Conclusions. The sustained production of factor VIII was observed after APOLT in a canine hemophilia model. Transplantation of approximately one third of the normal whole liver resulted in cure of hemophilia. Distinctive function of the partial liver graft coexisting with native liver was monitored by measuring blood factor VIII activity. These findings suggest the feasibility of APOLT as a curative treatment for patients with hemophilia. Background: The relationship between body mass index (BMI) and outcome of liver transplantation (OLT) has been debated, particulary in patients with hepatitis C (HCV). Aims: To examine the association between BMI and postOLT survival. Methods: Two NIH-sponsored multi-center databases prospectively enrolled OLT recipients at 4 highvolume centers. Data including preOLT BMI and liver disease diagnosis and postOLT follow-up were extracted in 2707 adult patients undergoing primary OLT between 1985 and 2000. The relationship between BMI and survival was assessed by the proportional hazards analysis. Results: The table describes patient characteristics prior to OLT. The mean BMI for the cohort was 26.4 kg/m 2 (SD 5.6). There was no difference in BMI between patients with HCV (n=647) and those without (n=2060). Subsequent to OLT, 734 recipients died. The figure shows that for the overall cohort, there was no association between preOLT BMI and the risk of postOLT death (95% confidence band also shown). Among patients with HCV, high BMI was not associated with shorter survival, whereas there was a trend for an increased risk of death at the lower end of BMI (p=0.055). In a multivariable analysis, BMI had no effect on survival, as compared to age (hazard ratio(HR)=1.02, p<0.01) and HCV (HR=1.34, p=0.01). No signficant interaction was present between BMI and HCV. Conclusions: In this large cohort of carefully selected OLT recipients at high-volume centers, BMI had no effect on postOLT survival. In particular, there was no association between increasing BMI and mortality in patients with HCV. These data suggest that high or low BMI alone should not be construed as contraindications for OLT even in patients with HCV. Aims: ARF requiring renal replacement therapy (RRT) either before or after LT may be associated with a poor outcome. We aimed to determine the changing incidence of RRT, identify risk factors for post LT requirement for RRT and the impact of RRT on patient/ graft survival. Methods: Prospectively collected data on all adult patients undergoing elective LT for chronic liver disease between 1994 and 2001 was analyzed. Combined liver and kidney transplants and fulminant hepatitis were excluded. Mann-Whitney U-test was used for quantitative data, Pearson's chi-square test for categorical data, Kaplan-Meier method for actuarial survival and Wilcoxon method for comparison. Results: 753 patients underwent LT for end stage disease of which 57 had pre existing renal impairment (S.creatinine > 150µmol/L). Post LT RRT was required in 117 of the 753 patients (15.5%). The incidence of post LT RRT increased from 9.6% in 1994 to 20.4% in 2001. Patients with normal pre LT renal function (n=696) were less likely to require post LT RRT (n=92,13%) compared to those with pre-existing renal impairment (n=57) who had a significantly higher risk of post LT RRT (n=25, 44%). Factors assessed for post LT RRT were divided into recipient, donor, intra operative and graft factors. Among recipient factors, patients who were in hospital at time of LT, pre LT ventilatory requirement (p=0.003), a higher BMI (<0.0001), abnormal Hb (p=0.001), WBC (p=0.01) and coagulation profile (p=0.01) were significant on univariate analysis. Donor age (47 vs 42, p=0.004) and BMI (p=0.01) was greater for those with post LT RRT. Intra-operative blood (p<0.0001), fresh frozen plasma (p<0.0001) and cryoprecipitate (p=0.002) use was significantly greater in those requiring RRT. Of 604 patients with normal renal function without RRT (Group A), 52 died in the first year (8.6%), 32 within three months. Of those with normal renal function with RRT (Group B, n=92), 52 (56.5%) died within the first year. Patients with pre LT renal impairment that improved post LT without RRT (Group C) (32) had a 1 yr mortality of 9.2% (n=3). Those with pre existing renal impairment who then required RRT (Group D) (n=25) also had a higher 1 yr mortality (n=8, 32%). Conclusions: The incidence of post LT renal impairment requiring RRT is increasing. Post LT RRT is associated with increased mortality, particularly in those with normal pre existing renal function. MMF + Tacro + CS therapy was associated with statistically significant reduction in graft loss and patient death vs. Tacro Introduction: Vascular endothelial growth factor (VEGF) is an endothelial cell mitogen and an important stimulator of sinusoidal endothelial cell proliferation. The aim of this research was to study the effects of exogenous VEGF in a rat model of acute liver failure. Materials and Methods:The study was conducted on 64 rats (240-300 gr.) according to the 1991 Revision of Guiding Principles in the Care and Use of Animals. All rats underwent intraperitoneal injection (5 ml/Kg) of 25% carbon tetrachloride (CCl 4 ) and 75% paraffin oil. This dosage of CCl 4 was devised to induce nonfatal acute liver failure with spontaneous recovery in 7 days. The animals were randomly divided into 2 groups. Group B animals underwent i.v. injection of 200 ng of VEGF 165 one hour following intra-peritoneal injection of CCl 4 . To obtain daily LFTs and histological liver samples, 4 rats in each group were sacrificed daily up to 8 days. Results: In group A the liver histology showed massive periportal hepatocyte necrosis associated with portal lymphocytic infiltrates. The peak of the damage was documented at 72 hours following CCl 4 . Group B showed minimal necrosis, moderate periportal edema and a minimum periportal steatosis. At 48 hours steatotic changes had disappeared and the periportal edema was resolving. LFTs demonstrated severe liver damage in rats in group A. In group A the peak AST (mean 322.5 IU/L) and ALT (mean 250.25 IU/L) were recorded at 72 hours. In group B, at 72 hours the mean AST was 137 IU/L (normal <95 IU/L) and ALT 68 IU/l (normal <45 IU/L). The maximum levels of AST and ALT, in group B, were 152.3 IU/L and 72.3 IU/L, at 24 hours. Conclusion: According to our results exogenous VEGF successfully protects the liver from CCl 4 induced acute liver failure. Further studies will demonstrate if exogenous VEGF can be effective in other liver injuries. Background: This study was undertaken to determine the causes of mortality in liver recipients who survived at least 10 years after transplantation. Methods: Retrospectively collected data of 455 consecutive patients (497 grafts) who received liver grafts between May 1977 and Sept. 1993 were reviewed, which included 74 pediatric patients (86 grafts); 381 adult patients (411 grafts). Demographics included age, gender, indications for transplantation, patient survival, graft survival and causes of death. Follow-up data was available for 96% of patients. Results: Two hundreds and thirty-nine patients survived more than 10 years. Recipients who survived the first year had 10-year survival of 68%. Twenty-nine patients died during their second decade post liver transplantation. The deaths occurred from10.2 years to 19.7 years after transplantation. 88% of the recipients who survived the first decade are still alive. Death with a functioning graft occurred in 20 patients (68%). The causes of death in those 20 recipients were de novo malignancy (n=9), cardiovascular disease and cerebrovascular disease (n=8), and infection (n=3). The mean age at death of patients who died from cardiovascular disease and malignancy was 63 years. The remaining 9 deaths were graft related which included recurrent disease (n=3), chronic rejection (n=2), and others (n=4). The mean age of patients who died of graft failure was 48.8 years. Ten patients (35%) died with impaired kidney function likely due to Cyclosporine toxicity. There was an increased risk of graft failure in male recipients of female livers. Biliary atresia, Primary Sclerosing Cholangitis and Primary biliary cirrhosis had the best 10 years survival rates (76, 65 and 59% respectively). Aside from three patients who died of recurrent disease, 4 patients who died of causes other than recurrent disease had dysfunction from recurrent disease. Conclusion: Survival of liver recipients who live beyond the 10 th post-transplant year is very good. De-novo malignancy emerges as the major cause of death follwed by cardiovascular disease and cerebral vascular accidents. Aggressive monitoring for cardiovascular disease and stringent follow-up surveillance to enable early detection of de-novo cancers may further improve long term outcome. Background: The National Liver Transplantation Programme in the UK was established in 1983 and since then over 9000 transplants have taken place. The aim of this study was to investigate the long-term transplant survival of these liver transplants. Methods: Data on elective liver transplants performed in the eight designated liver centres in the UK from 1 January 1985 to 31 December 2002 were obtained from the National Transplant Database (NTxD). Long-term transplant survival estimates were obtained using the Kaplan-Meier method. Transplant survival time was calculated as time from first graft to patient death or graft failure. Results: Between 1985 and 2002, 6197 first cadaveric heartbeating liver only transplants were performed: 5369 (87%) in adult and 828 (13%) in paediatric patients. Transplant survival estimates and corresponding 95% confidence intervals (CI) were 63% (CI 62-65%) at 5 years, 52% (CI 51-54%) at 10 years, 42% (CI 38-46%) at 15 years and 38% (CI 33-44%) at 18 years post-transplant. The median transplant survival time was 11 years (95% CI 10.2-12.4 years). There was some evidence to suggest better long-term transplant survival for paediatric recipients compared with adult recipients: 56% and 35%, respectively, of transplants still functioning after 18 years, p=0.06. Analysing these data by year of transplant (in three-year groups from 1985 to 2002) showed that transplant survival was significantly different between the groups, p<0.0001, with more recent transplants doing better. This was mainly due to a significant improvement in transplant survival in the first year post-transplant for the years analysed. One year transplant survival was 54% (95% CI 48-59%) for transplants in 1985 to 1987 compared with 85% (95% CI 83-87%) for transplants in 2000 to 2002, p<0.0001. Analysis of longterm transplant survival of those transplants functioning after one year showed no statistically significant effect of transplant year. Conclusions: 50% of first cadaveric heartbeating liver only transplants recorded on the NTxD since 1985 were still functioning after 11 years. Long-term transplant survival has significantly improved over the last 18 years. This improvement has been principally due to fewer graft losses in the first post-transplant year. However, there has been no appreciable improvement in transplant survival for those transplants still functioning after one year.  Living-donor liver transplantation (LDLT) is a universally accepted therapy for patients with chronic liver disease. Experience with the use of live donors (LD) in fulminant hepatic failure (FHF) is limited. Goal: To review our experience with LDLT in adults and children with FHF. Methods: From 06/95 to 12/03, 40 consecutive children (mean age of 3.5 years, 63% females) with FHF underwent LT: 21 with deceased donors (DD) and 19 (47.5%) with LD. Twenty two consecutive adults (mean age of 33 years, 100% females) with FHF were transplanted from 02/00 (date of the 1 st LDLT in our center) to 12/03: 15 with DD and 7 (32%) with LD. Etiology of FHF in the 19 children who underwent LDLT was indeterminate in 13 (68%), HAV in 4 (21%), autoimmune hepatitis in 1 and toxic in 1. Among the 7 adults, 3 had autoimmune hepatitis (43%), 2 HBV (29%), 1 hepatotoxicity and 1 FHF of indeterminate etiology. All patients with FHF were listed for DD. At the same time, one or more LD were evaluated. LDLT was performed whenever a patient deteriorated and no DD became available. The use of LDLT in FHF was approved by the Institutional Ethics Committee. Results: Actuarial 1-year survival was 76% and 84% in children and 73% and 71% in adults transplanted with DD or LD respectively. Among the 7 adults, pre-LT serum bilirubin was 17.1±7 mg/dL, INR 3.5±2.2 and median ALT 1096 IU/L (428-7139 IU/L). Two patients with deep coma and cerebral edema were referred from other institutions after 7 days of waitlist for DD. Both died within 24 hours of LDLT from multiorgan failure. In the remaining 5 patients who survived, LDLT was indicated at the time when encephalopathy progressed to stage 3 in 2 patients and to stage 4 in 3. The median time-interval to LDLT in survivors was 3 days (2-4 days). Conclusions: 1) LDLT is at the present time a normal practice in our center for children with FHF; 2) To be successful, LDLT among adults with FHF should be indicated in a timely fashion and early in the course of the disease. Freeman 1 . 1 Transplant Surgery, Tufts-New England Medical Cener, Boston, MA, USA Background: Mycophenolic acid (MPA), cyclosporine (CSA) and corticosteroids (CS), are commonly used immunosuppressant drugs in clinical transplantation. In addition, CSA suppressed TNFa production from TNFa CGP1 cells significantly more than in TNFa CGP2 cells. CS significantly suppressed TNFa production from cells containing IL-1RaCGP1 relative to IL-1RaCGP2 cells. TNFa production from the TNFa CGP1 cells is significantly suppressed by CS during PBMC proliferation compared with TNFa CGP2 cells. Conclusions: Our data suggest that some CGPs or combinations are associated with higher TNFa production during PMBC proliferation. Some of these are less sensitive to immunosuppression. This suggests that some CGPs or combinations (genotypes) deserve further study and may help to identify candidates for immunosuppressive reduction and/or withdrawal. Introduction: Recent reports demonstrate the efficacy of induction immunosuppression with Thymoglobuline, a potent anti-thymocyte policlonal antibody, in allowing alloengraftment and acquired tolerance, by means of a tolerogenic regimen of recipient pre-treatment and low-dose postoperative immunosuppression. We investigated the effects of this novel approach in a series of consecutive 53 liver transplant patients after a followup of > 1 year, and the possibility of weaning off immunosuppression. Methods: from October 2002, we used Thymoglobuline pre-treatment (5 mg/kg once-only i.o. dose) + low-dose Tacrolimus monotherapy in 53 patients (19 LDLT, 34 CAD-LT) receiving liver transplantation for various indications. Results: Cumulative patient's 1-year survival rate was 87%, not significantly different from an historical control group. 25 patients (47%) experienced 32 mild acute rejection episodes, all treated with a 1-g bolus of Methylprednisolone; 4 patients (7.5%) required steroid recycle, 2 OKT3 treatment; rejection rate was similar in control group (41%) but 26.9 % episodes were graded moderate or severe, and required steroid recycle+maintenance (30.9% pts) or OKT3 treatment (10.2%). Mean TAC levels were 7.1 ng/ml (range < 1.5-13.6 ng/ml) (10.4 ng/ml control group, p=0.04), whereas mean daily TAC doses were 4.2 vs 8.4 (p=0.01); after a mean period of 4.2±1.4 months, a planned weaning schedule was initiated: 26 Thymoglobuline pts stay in BID TAC administration, 11 pts OD, 3 pts every other day, 2 pts 3 times/week, 1 pt once/ monthly; 3 pts were discontinued from TAC for toxicity and stay on Sirolimus OD monotherapy. No major rejection complications were experienced subsequent to weaning. Conclusion: Induction with Thymoglobuline in liver transplant recipients is effective in protecting against rejection and can actively promote graft tolerance and gradual weaning off immunosuppression, the actual dosages and levels of which are far lower than historically considered. Villamil 1 . 1 Liver Unit, Fundacion Favaloro, Buenos Aires, Argentina The key for success in fulminant hepatic failure (FHF) is to identify on admission patients who will survive with medical therapy and those who will require urgent liver transplantation (LT). The King's College and Clichy's prognostic criteria have been previously validated. MELD has been found to be an excellent predictor of mortality among cirrhotics awaiting LT. At each of the four time points during liver transplantation, two blood samples were obtained. Points of measurement included: baseline (after anesthetic induction and before incision), five minutes prior to reperfusion (III-5), ten minutes after reperfusion (III+10), and seventy minutes after reperfusion (III+70). One sample was sent for routine PT and TEG analysis. In the second sample, 6.1 mcg of rFVIIa was added (approximate equivalent dose of 120 mcg/kg for an average sized patient) before PT and TEG analysis. PT was measured in seconds. TEG variables included reaction time, clot formation rate, maximum amplitude (MA), and lysis at 30 minutes. Data at all four time points were analyzed using the Wilcoxon signed rank test. Results: Nine of 10 patients had PT and TEG analysis performed at all 4 time points. The tenth patient had the baseline analysis only, due to intraoperative complications. There was a statistically significant difference in the baseline PT (P<0.02, 15.8 ± 3.21 seconds in the routine sample and 13.6 ± 2.36 seconds in the rFVIIa sample). There was a statistically significant difference in the TEG MA (P<0.02) at 5 minutes prior to reperfusion. The mean MA in the routine sample was 53.5 mm, and was 39 in the rFVIIa sample. There were no other statistically significant differences in the PT or in the TEG variables at any of the other sampling times. Conclusions: Previous studies have shown a change in TEG variables as well as PT with in vivo administration of rFVIIa. It does not appear that an in vitro model will permit assessment of the effect of rFVIIa prior to its administration to a patient. Situs inversus totalis (SIT) is rare and involves right-left reversal of the abdominal and thoracic viscera. Tetralogy of Fallot (TOF) is associated with early mortality without surgical repair. Both present perioperative management challenges. We report the first case of living-donor liver transplant (LDLT) to an adult with SIT and TOF. A 62 year-old man with SIT, TOF, and primary sclerosing cholangitis presented for LDLT. TOF resulted in a reconstructed RV outflow tract, ventricular septal defect closure, and pulmonary valve homograft. Tricuspid annuloplasty was also performed. Preoperative ECG had normal sinus rhythm with right bundle branch block. Echocardiogram revealed pulmonary and tricuspid regurgitation, RV dilation with reduced function, and LV hypertrophy with normal function. Coronary angiography showed mild atherosclerosis. Intraoperative ECG leads were reversed due to the dextrocardia. Transesophageal echocardiography was used to guide a pulmonary artery catheter past the repaired tricuspid annulus, and assess preload and ventricular function. The operation was hemodynamically uneventful. Postoperatively, he had 2 episodes of SVT with spontaneous conversion to sinus rhythm. Twelve days post-discharge, he developed reentrant SVT requiring administration of adenosine. He was placed on atenolol and has had no more episodes of SVT. There are several issues to consider in LDLT with SIT and TOF: The postreperfusion syndrome during orthotopic liver transplantations is defined as a decrease in the mean arterial pressure of 30% to 70% below its baseline value, and can occur within 1 minute of the graft's reperfusion. The postreperfusion syndrome is often severe, can cause early graft dysfunction and may culminate in multi-organ failure in the recipient. The precise mechanism of the postreperfusion syndrome is not known. Inotropes and vasopressors have been used to counteract the often profund hypotension of the postreperfusion syndrome, but were not able to prevent its occurence. There is evidence, however, that the postreperfusion syndrome is caused by the influx of oxygen free radicals, generated in the liver graft during its ischemic time, into the circulation of the recipient at the reperfusion of the liver graft. Based on this assumption, mannitol which besides its well known diuretic functions has also radical scavenging properties, can be employed to attenuate or even prevent the occurence of the postreperfusion syndrome. We report about the successful use of mannitol for the prevention of the postreperfusion syndrome in more than 200 patients undergoing orthotopic liver transplantations. About 10 minutes prior to reperfusion of the liver graft, a bolus of mannitol 20%, 1 g/kg, is infused into the recipients circulation to be present as radical scavenger at the moment of reperfusion of the liver graft. If necessary, boluses of calcium chloride are given to elevate the calcium level > 1 mmol/l, and sodium bicarbonate is infused to correct the base excess towards +/ -0 prior to reperfusion. At reperfusion of the liver graft, an infusion of mannitol 20%, 1g/ kg, is started for the duration of 1 hour for continuous radical scavenging from the reperfused liver graft. Vasopressors and/or inotropes are used during the reperfusion phase only if the mean arterial blood pressure drops below 70 mmHg. With this algorithm we have been able to completely prevent the postreperfusion syndrome from occuring in the majority of orthotopic liver transplantations, and had to resort to a short term use of vasopressors and/ or inotropes in only a few of the cases. While intraoperative potassium disturbances during liver transplantation are well described, their importance in patients undergoing liver retransplantation, a population with substantial perioperative challenges and suboptimal outcomes, has not been studied. The aims of the present study were to investigate the incidence and risk factors of intraoperative potassium disturbances in liver retransplantation. After IRB approval, the medical records at a single tertiary academic center between January 1, 1998 and June 30, 2003 were reviewed. During the study period, 137 patients underwent 157 retransplantations. Three patients were excluded from analysis because of incomplete data. Of 134 patients, 130 patients received a second graft, 21 patients a third and three patients a fourth. On average, 5.9 potassium values per operation were recorded. Overall, 106 patients (68.8%) developed hyperkalemia (K > 5.0 mmol/L), hypokalemia (K < 3.5 mmol/L) or combined disturbance (hyperkalemia and hypokalemia). The range of potassium values was 2.2 mmol/L to 8.0 mmol/L. Of 154 retransplantations, 48 patients (31.2%) developed hyperkalemia, 43 patients (27.9%) hypokalemia and 15 patients (9.7%) combined disturbance. In the hyperkalemic group, 24 patients (15.6%) developed severe hyperkalemia with the potassium value exceeding 6.0 mmol/L. Potassium elevation occurred in the prereperfusion period in 14 patients, in the postreperfusion period in 17 patients and in both the pre and postreperfusion periods in 18. In pediatric patients (age 18 or less, n=43), hypokalemia (48.8%) was more common than hyperkalemia (4.7%), or combined potassium disturbance (13.9%). Preliminary analysis (ANOVA) revealed that older age, higher preoperative serum creatinine and potassium levels, but not blood urea nitrogen, prothrombin time were significantly associated with intraoperative hyperkalemia during liver retransplantation. In conclusion, intraoperative potassium disturbances, either hyperkalemia or hypokalemia, are common in patients undergoing liver retransplantation; intraoperative hyperkalemia is associated with older age, higher preoperative serum creatinine and potassium levels. Further classification and modification of preoperative risk factors may improve the outcome of liver retransplantation. Flavio Takaoka No epidural catheters were placed in the last 55 donors.
